A TRAGEDY.
ACT 1 : “I consider Crick to be by far the cleverest fellow on the Opposition benches—in fact, he’s the only man there who knows anything about anything.”—PARKES.
ACT 2 : “I move that Crick be expelled Parliament because he’s too cheeky.”—PARKES.
ACT 3 : “We find that Crick died of the disease known in America as Big Head, said disease having arisen from an overdose of marmalade administered by Henry Parkes with
malice aforethought.”— THE JURY.

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted -
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say SOOO words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Neicspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
“ The Bulletin" will return all unavailable MSS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.

for a year.

city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, hut is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom ice do not ivish
to interfere.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 22, 1890.
A Political Three-Card Trick.
The new and fearfully-wonderful Electoral
Bill formulated by the New South Wales
Cabinet is a tacit acknowledgment that the
men of the colony are growing exceedingly
tired of Pabkes, and consequently that wily
cultivator of the field of human sympathy
has resolved to throw himself on the feminine
population, in the hope that they may be a
shade less weary than their husbands and
fathers. For some undefined and unfixable
reason it has always been assumed that the
old politician has his strongest supporters
among the female sex. His hair is in some
measure responsible for this impression, and
possibly his verse has also something to do
with it, though even Pabkes can hardly
hope to travel for many years on these two
unstable assets. But the male electors have
shown unmistakable signs of wearying of
him, and the women of New South
Wales at the worst can do no more than
throw him out ; so should the old man’s
last political dodge fail to save him at the
next general election, he will at least fall in
an entirely new capacity, as a democrat who
was too advanced for his age, instead of
coming down with a dull, ordinary thud as
nobody in particular. The ruling passion of
Pabkes’ whole career is conspicuous in this
measure from the first line to the last; like
every other prominent device of his political
existence, it is borrowed from an opponent,
without acknowledgment. The democratic
principle of One Man One Vote has been the
rock ahead for the Freetrade and Bloody
Shirt faction ever since that motley gather-
ing came into existence. The party has
lived on the multiplied suffrages of the
importers and landholders of Sydney ; and
it has flourished because the pimpled
publican, and the pugilist who has
made a pile by assault and battery, and
the mining shark and the successful
“ shindykit” have been allowed to exercise
from two to twenty-five votes a-piece, while
the person who owns nothing but his labour
and his brains has possessed only one. But
this political monopoly has grown precarious
in recent years, and the Freetrade gospel has
fallen upon evil days. The nation grew
Aveary of shoddy, and the Yellow Pup was
frayed aAvay by the sloav friction of Time
and the concussion of public scorn, and an
impression grew and spread that the faith
from Derry might still survive even if
McMillan’, r percentage fell away, and that
Abigail might fall and the Kingdom of
Heaven might remain just Avhere it was. And
finally, by the time that Mr. Traill’s measure
for the establishment of manhood suffrage and
the abolition of the Freetrade plural vote had
struggled successfully through its second
reading, it became evident that the abolition
of plural voting was only another name for
the abolition of Pabkes himself. Therefore
that astute politician proposes to hang his
party to avoid execution, and by his scheme
he stands a reasonable chance to escape the
general calamity which threatens to swamp
his followers.
» * *
By adopting his present course and going
back on all his previous policy, Sir Henry
gains four distinct advantages. When The
Bulletin first formulated the idea of One
Man One Vote, it became a rallying cry
for the democracy of New South Wales, and
when Parkes has discovered that he can no
longer oppose or retard this great re-
form he unblushingly annexes it as
his own, just as he annexed the famous
Education Act against which he had fought
with sectarian frenzy and bigotry for years.
Should the Electoral Bill pass into law,
therefore, he will trade upon it as the
greatest and most brilliant act of his long
career, and the fact that the idea was stolen
from his opponents, and the inspiration
borrowed from his enemies, will be judi-
ciously dropped into oblivion. For the
moment Sir Henry poses, as a democrat to
catch the democratic vote which now
threatens to wipe him out, and so far his
position is a good one. But in addition to
this he has enlarged the scope of his
borrowed scheme, and having grafted
Womanhood on to Manhood Suffrage he will
assume the position of the one really
advanced statesman of the country, and will
look with an air of pathetic reproach upon
the party whose scheme he passes oft’ as his
own, and will lament about the narrowness
of a democracy which is not sufficiently
democratic to keep pace with the age. The
part is one to which the actor is admirably
suited ; should occasion demand the sacrifice
Sir Henry Parkes would probably assume
the command of the Protectionist party on
the ground that no other Protectionist was
sufficiently protective for the situation, or he
would grasp the Catholic vote an bloc , and
capsize Cardinal Moran off his throne on
suspicion of being a disguised Orangeman,
or at least a person whose want of religious
fervour was undermining the glory of the
Holy Roman Church. A boundless capacity
for going the wdiole hog, quite regardless of
whose hog he may go, is Parkes’ chief
political stock-in-trade, and for the hundredth
time this venerable device promises to save
him from imminent destruction.
* * *
Also, there are two other contingencies in
view, and in either or both of them Parkes
comes uppermost. The principle of man-
hood suffrage is urgently demanded by
the country, but the demand for woman-
hood suffrage is strictly limited. Conse-
quently, the Electoral Bill—which has been
adopted by the importers’ party under pres-
sure of necessity, on the ground that suicide
is an easier death than execution—may very
probably go the way the great Dead House
scheme went, and vanish into the same
obscurity which swallowed up the project
for embezzling the name of this continent
and thereby mixing up the geography of the
Southern Hemisphere past human compre-
hension. Then the Free trade faction will
gain a new lease of its precarious existence,
and Parkes will reap all the advantages of
being a Democrat without suffering any of
the consequences ; and he will be supported
by the multiplied vote of the traders and
landlords as of old, partly because they have
no other capable leader, and partly because
they will owe their very existence to the man
who smothered a possible democratic measure
by introducing another which was impos-
sible. By tacking Womanhood on to Man-
hood Suffrage the chances of the latter are
diminished at least 90 per cent., and if the
old politician had ventured to add a clause
providing for a general slaughter of infants
he would have thereby demolished the last
hope of placing his measure upon the statute-
book, and the Foreign-trade party would
have rested in peace. As it is, he has done
everything in his power to make his demo-
cratic project distasteful even to the demo-
cratic politicians from whom it has been
stolen, and when that party throws out its
own mangled bill Sir Henry will call upon
the democracy to support him against the
ignorant conservatism of their own leaders,
and thus both sides will owe him a tre-
mendous debt of gratitude, and unless the
old man is in the last stages of mental decay
he will collect the Bill religiously from each
of his creditors and give not one of them a
receipt. And, lastly, in the almost impos-
sible event of the Electoral Bill becoming
law, the Foreign-trade aggregation will still
support Parkes on the ground that he
did his best to smother his own scheme and
for the rest he will trust to the gratitude of
the enfranchised women of the colony to
vote down the male democracy—in other
words, he will travel on his hair and his
alleged poetry once more, with the off-chance
that the last vestiges of the worn-out Kiama
Ghost may carry him through. As a plan
for furnishing him with a solid claim upon
everybody, and yet furnishing nobody with
any sort of a claim upon him, the Electoral
Bill is an invention which has few equals in
modern politics.
# # *
The ostensible purpose and object of this
measure is the only feature in the case which
is of no immediate consequence. The en-
franchisement of woman, despite all the
clamour and dust which have been raised
about the subject, is a matter of only second-
rate importance, and when she is enfranchised
the effect upon politics will be infinitesimal.
The objections against female suffrage are
hardly worth recapitulation. It is claimed
that even in the most intelligent communi-
ties women are hopelessly ignorant of public
matters, and are, therefore, incapable of
using the suffrage to any possible advantage
—that they are conservative by nature, and
would vote for anything with a title and a
glittering exterior —and that they are led by
words and phrases, and are prepared to
support any charlatan who choses to flatter
their vanity and trade upon their folly. And
no doubt these assertions are all more or less
true, but as they are equally true of the male
community, the next disaster will be only the
old one over again. For more than bO years
the electors of New South Wales have voted
for Farkes because he was large and
pompous and loud-mouthed, and having
been sold by him on every possible occasion
throughout his career they continue to vote
for him all the same. They have believed
in his Kiama Ghost because he told
them to do so, and when he informed them
that the Lord was interested in the cause of
Foreign-trade, and that the voice of Provi-
dence was engaged on behalf of Abigail’s
loan office and Brunker’s auctioneering
business, they accepted the information with
tho calm, unreasoning faith of the naked
cannibal'who is taken in by his missionary.
They accepted the Treason Felony Act when
the Ministry of which Farkes was a mem-
ber passed that infamous device upon the
nation, and, they also accepted his after
statement that lie had no part or share in
the matter. They have allowed him to mas-
querade as the author of the Education Law
regardless of the notorious fact that he first
opposed it, then borrowed it from his
opponent, and last of all repudiated the
men whose ideas he had plagiarised.
They have voted for Freetrade land-
lords because they made hazy promises
about land-nationalisation, and they have
rallied up to support one foreign-trader
because he was a teetotaller, and another
because he was interested in football —in
short, they have habitually advertised for
someone to take them in, and their unwhole-
some desire to be swindled has been gratified
to tho utmost. If the female voter of New
South “Wales —should that person ever come
into existence —can break this record of
comprehensive stupidity then she suffers
from a mental deficiency that is past all
remedy ; and if she votes for Farkes be-
cause of his hair she will stand exactly on
the same level as the male elector who votes
for him because of his size and the looseness
of his facts. Feminine suffrage is only a
comparatively unimportant side issue at the
best ; the real question to be decided is
whether an exceedingly slippery politician
will succeed for the hundredth time in
borrowing his enemy’s brains and passing
them oil' on an ignorant nation as his own.
And, considering that Sir Henry Farkes
has for many years made borrowing a fine
art The Bulletin is inclined to the opinion
that if the old man is really on the job he
will succeed in discounting his mental pro-
missory note as before.
The Ingratitude of Labour.
Mr. H. H. Champion, in his capacity as the
friend of Capital, publishes in the Age a column
of wild rejoicing over the collapse of the great
strike, and heaps all the resources of his cul-
tured Billingsgate on the Australian workmen
because of their defeat. In other words, he
hollers for the dog that is uppermost, and his
reasons for doing this are expressed— so far as
he knows how—in the following words :
Nor is this, bitter as it must be to all Australians, the
bitterest fruit of this bad business. Just see what is the
certain result of this failure, a failure caused first by the
arrogant imbecility of the strike leaders, notably, so far
as Victoria is concerned, Messrs. Hancock, Murphy, and
Trenwith, and, secondly, by the slavishness of the rank
and lile who preferred to be blind followers of the blind
rather than face the flood of scurrility which is let loose
on every critic. You workmen in Australia have dashed
the hopes of all men in the world who have worked and
watched and waited in the belief that when he had
material prosperity and leisure the labouring man would
prove worthy to rule the earth wisely and justly.
One day last May I went to see an old Chartist who, I
had heard, was dying in poverty and neglect in a bye
street near the Middlesex Hospital in London. He told
me then something of his life for the last 45 years—desti-
tution, discouragement, struggle, effort, always futile and
of ,en mistaken, but ever illumined by the hope that the
world was nearing the dawn of the day of freedom and
justice, approaching the emancipation of man from the
thraldom of oppression and penury, lust, and ignorance,
which is the “far-off divine event to which the whole
creation moves.” How he envied me the youth that
gave me a, chance of living to see the Promised Land
denied to him! How he forecold that in Australia I
should find that the political democracy, the free educa-
tion, the high wages, which had been his lifelong desire,
had reared a race of workmen, brave, capable, self-re-
strained, gentle !
This old Chartist is a type of thousands all over the
world—not the men who make the speeches and are
interviewed by the papers and get into Parliament—not
the Broadhursts, Liebknechts, Lafargues or Powderleys,
but the thousands who carry the banners and form the
processions and pay the pence in the vague hope that,
perhaps, the men on the Pisgah platforms can point them
the way out of the wilderness of want and woe. On the
hearts of these thousands, from the sand lots of San
Francisco to the salt mines of Siberia, a chill will have
fallen when the message Is flashed all over the planet that
this misguided strike has ended in collapse and dishonour.
For this you Australian workmen have to thank your
leaders and your own lack of courage.
* * *
If there is any meaning at all in these
sentences they are intended as a declaration
that the Australian democracy has no longer
any reasonable object to strive after—that it has
already attained an industrial Paradise, and
any further effort is only a sign of ignorant
discontent and a purposeless striving after
anarchy. Mr. Champion’s Chartist, dying
miserably on his bed of straw in poverty,
hunger and neglect, chose to regard Australia
as the workman’s elysium, because the Austra-
lian, so far as he knew him, had secured many
ad van t iges which the wage-slave of Britain is
still denied, therefore, as lie is a shade better
off than the English toiler, the Australian must
seek no further advancement and have no
further ambitions. The imported puppet with
the dress-suit and the eye-glass has 1 00 W,1
through all the ages of futurity, and has di
covered that progress, so far as Labour is con
cerned, has reached its climax in this couni
and for the future the masses must lie low
be at rest; and therefore he seeks t
squash any further movement on the part f
an unreasonable nation. And yet his creed °
woefully hollow and imbecile after all m/ 8
Australian hm gained all these things which 16
in the narrow estimate of the ignorant Chartist
of England—are required to make the life of tl 1
workman a joyous vision of happiness and
affluence, and his continued dissatisfaction
doubt seems to that same unlettered democrat—
who only comprehends the first beginnings f
democracy—a strangely incomprehensible* cir
cumstance. But the English workman livin
in his attic, and sleeping on his bed of straw*
has already got more material prosperity and
more political liberty than have ever entered
into the wildest dreams of many a starvin /
Chinese agriculturist or Indian ryot, and his
chronic discontent would probably be to them
fully as unfathomable; yet even the Chinaman
and the ryot are not quite at the bottom of the
social scale, and here and there on the earth’s
surface may be found sundry naked tribes who
eke out a precarious existence on dirt and bark
and decayed fish, and who would look upon the
hungriest Mongol in the barren wastes of the
Siberian border as a person occupying an
enviable pinnacle of wealth and prosperity
The astounded British dude who has posed hi
Australia as an apostle of democracy, is no
doubt sincere in his own humble stupidity
when he asks why the Australian should be
discontented, seeing that he already possesses
nearly everything that the Englishman is still
striving to. acquire ; and the penniless Pole
who comes into London to look for a job hi
some sweater’s den, would probably ask the
Englishman the same question, and find it
equally hard to get an answer.
# # #
The Australian workman enjoys the triple
advantages of liberal education, high wages
and short hours —that is to say, his education
is more liberal, his wages are higher, and his
hours shorter than those of his class in many
other countries —and for these reasons Mr.
Champion expected to find in turn an ideal
toiler, “ brave, capable, self-restrained and
gentle.” As the Australian fails to come up
to this standard, however, the British prophet
curses at large, and reproaches the nation for
its unreasoning ingratitude. Yet the capitalist’s
hours are still shorter, and his education is still
more liberal, and his profits fifty times larger,
and still these advantages have produced no
corresponding moral improvement in him, nor,
apparently, does anyone expect that they should.
Several eminent British landlords now living
count their incomes by hundreds of thousands
per annum, but the steady increase in their
takings leads to no conspicuous regeneration, and
one of the great proprietors of London, who
now collects some £400,000 yearly, has a fair
prospect of increasing his income during the
next 20 years to neardy £1,000.000, by further
rack-renting his wretched tenants, and yet
there is no visible cause to expect that the
additional £600,000 will elevate his morals or
lessen his rapacity. But while £l2OO per diem
is a conspicuous failure as an agency for im-
proving the human soul, the capitalist still
demands that 50s. a week should produce a
saintly and philosophic toiler, and when the
recipient of tins princely income expresses him-
self dissatisfied, and asks for more, he is filled
with disgust at the unbounded thanklessness of
the human race.
# * *
The self-constituted friend of Labour is, un-
happily, a person who sees only one side of the
great problem. The Australian tradesman,
when fully employed, earns sufficient to keep
him in food, lodging, and clothing, and there-
fore the condescending Socialist with the
jewellery and the white waistcoat, who came
out to dry-nurse him in his difficulties, expected
that he would be contented with his lot. And
if the workman were a horse whose ambitions
were limited by a stable and an abundance of
oats the expectation might be reasonable enough.
But the theory that wages should be measured
by the amount required to keep the wage-
earner in working condition is exploded beyond
repair. There is no more I’eason why the
artizan should receive just enough to enable
him to continue at his calling than there is
that the duke should be limited to that allow-
ance of food and clothing which will fit him to
bear up against the labour of being a
drone. The tradesman who now earns 50s. a
week, and wants more, may some years hence
be in receipt of £5 a week, and if so, the
chances are that he will still want more—in
fact, it would be exceedingly surprising if he
did not. The capitalist who owns £lOO,OOO is
grabbing for another £lOO,OOO with the same
remorseless rapacity which distinguished him
in the first beginnings of his prosperity, and
the landlord whose patch of ground on
Circular Quay or Collins-street is worth
half-a-million is as eager to boom his pro-
perty as if he were only an impoverished
Chinaman with an acre of unprofitable cabbages,
and the speculator who is realising 20 per cent,
is howling for something more per cent, as
hungrily as if he got no per-centage at all, and
the merchant who made a fortune by cornering
oats last year goes about like a roaring lion m
search of a chance to make a corner in beans
this year and realise another fortune. Y®t
nobody expects any of these individuals to be
satisfied with their wealth, or dreams that the
person whose land is leased at £5O per 100
frontage will not promptly raise that impost to
£6O whenever the opportunity presents itself,
and nobody is shocked when the person "ho
owns £60,000 proceeds to accumulate another
£50,000, and wants more even then. The rul e
that the demands of mankind should be linntec
to food, clothes, and house accommodation on a
reasonable scale is applied only to the class
which produces the World’s wealth, and when
the workman refuses to be the only satishe
being on earth, and the only one which lias no
ambitions beyond the wants of the momen ,
then that superior animal which calls itself t m
friend of labour is stunned by his monstrous
ingratitude, and abandons him in disgust.


r A Miss Mary Reed, a China missionary, who
lately arrived in Australia, has, with the aid of an
attendant parson, induced a number of simple-
minded enthusiasts to throw up their regular
occupations and start for China, there to engage in
the Sisyphean task of trying to “convert ” the
children of the sun from a faith which was ancient
2000 years 8.0. The names are : Miss Lloyd.
Miss Aspinall, Miss E. Box, Mr. O. Burgess, from
Melbourne ; Messrs. A. S. Dev enish, A. C.
Rogers and F. Burden, from Adelaide; Miss
Fysli, Miss M. Sorrenson and E. Steel, from Tas-
mania ; and Miss Booth from Sydney. The party
sailed from Sydney by the Menmuir last Satur-
day. The Bulletin hopes to see the time when,
there being still a single distressed person in
Australasia, the act of soliciting subscriptions
in aid of such an absurd scheme as a mission to
the distant Chinaman, will be made either a
criminal offence or the basis <*£ proceedings in
lunacy.
The business of such “ sympathisers ” as
Mr Champion is to act as the individual
j collective Uncle Pumblechook of the
Democracy, and the Democracy is resolved
to struggle through without an uncle, having
learned from experience that that relative is
neither useful nor ornamental. The Labour
party has made many mistakes in the past, and
will probably make many more in the future,
but it must find its own way, and declines to
be wheeled to victory in a perambulator by any
self-constituted patron. It lias committed a
serious error in confining itself too exclusively
to maintaining its position in relation to Capital,
whereas it should have aimed boldly at the
extinction of the capitalist and the establish-
ment of co-operation ; and it has failed to re-
cognise that the abolition of landlordism is
absolutely essential to its success, and instead of
making open war against every species of mono-
poly it has attacked only one—and that the
least important —of its enemies. But the demo-
cratic cause is only just begun, and if there is
any truth in the speculations of astronomy, it
has several thousand centuries before it in
which to repair the blunders of its infancy.
It has gained to-day nearly everything that
it demanded at the outset of its career ;
and. the new demands which it makes to-day
will he satisfied at some future time ; and when
they are satisfied the mental horizon of the
people will have still further enlarged, and the
Democracy will find new objects and new
aspirations. The laws of Nature forbid that
there should be any finality to such a move-
ment, and the bogus philosophers who lament
because the masses are still craving for further
progress might as well weep because Capital
still hungers after 10 per cent., or because the
world at large, despite the knowledge it has
acquired, continues to harass itself in the search
for new information, or because ambition in
every form is not dead and forgotten. The fact
that the Australian workman has already gained
nearly every concession which the Englishman
is still struggling to obtain, has no more bearing
on the matter than a curate has upon the stars.
The insolvent British tramp complains about
his rags, regardless of the circumstance that
some 250,000,000 of the human species possess
no rags at all, and the remorseless rules of logic
apply as well to one case as to the other.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Tory on Slavery.
The Saturday Review, a prominent organ of
the bigoted Toryism of England, publishes an
elaborate article on the inalienable right of one
man to toil and be hungry for the benefit of
another man who does not toil and is fat, and
sums up its argument in the following compre-
hensive sentence :
Why not acknowledge boldly that slavery of a kind-
call it what you will, and regulate it as you like—is a
necessary condition of political society ?
Why not, indeed, so long as the proprietor of
the Review belongs to the class of the slave-
owners, and not to that of the slaves ? The old
gag that the Briton is not a serf is long since
exploded —he is free to starve, but he is free in
no other way. Slavery is necessary—from the
Saturday Review standpoint—for if 99 per
cent, of the population were not slaves in some
shape or other the remaining 1 per cent, could
not loaf in palaces and do nothing, and as the
one great purpose of Providence, in the opinion
of the latter class, is that they should loaf in
palaces, then why not have slavery ? The
Roman patrician used to throw a live serf into
his fish-pond as food for the lampreys who made
a dainty dish for his dinner, and when a
meddlesome emperor prohibited this good old
custom he was a badly aggravated patrician,
and considered that his liberty w r as unjustly in-
terfered with. Like the Saturday Review,
he acknowledged that it might be unpleasant
for the serf who was eaten alive, but the
exigencies of a Roman nobleman’s dinner
demanded the sacrifice, and the whole
affair was ‘ ‘ a necessary condition of poli-
tical society.” In France, in the ancient
days of feudalism, there existed a statute which
set forth with much grotesque language and
quaint spelling that “a Seigneur, when he
comes home weary from Hunting, may kill not
more than two Serfs to comfort his Feet with
their warm Blood and Bowels,” and this was
also “a necessary condition of society ” in the
opinion of one party to the transaction, until
the other party rose up in a homicidal mood,
and declined to supply the blood and bowels
any longer. The other party is always the
stumbling-block in any such arrangement. He
persistently refuses to look upon the matter
from the same standpoint as his owner, and
even the unspeakable boss-fool of the Saturday
Review will sooner or later have his attention
drawn to this circumstance. The brutal arro-
gance of the Tory journalist assumes that if
there is to be slavery he is always to be the
slave-owner, and his well-fed Christian content-
ment enables him to bear with resignation the
fact that somebody else is condemned to life-
long drudgery. The idea that the serf may
revolt some day, and may harness the duke to
the mud-cart, and send up a monarch for seven
days because he has no honest means of support
is the other side of the picture ; and if the
Democracy at some future period endorses the
Re view’s ideas on the subject of slavery by
fastening the editor to a dray, and setting the
proprietor to carry bricks, it will be a sufficient
evidence that the ideas of that paper have not
been altogether thrown away.

The person who contributes London gossip to
the Argus is evidently a careful student of
that journal’s doctrines, and can be trusted to
work morsels of the true gospel into his weekly
letter. For example, lie writes that “ Sir
Somebody’s baronetcy is a glaring instance of
the absurdity of conferring hereditary honours
for inertly personal merit.” Now, to the un-
sophisticated Radical it appears that, whereas
the system of bestowing any sort of social title
upon any man is shameful, the “Crown’s”
least unpardonable excuse for ennobling unborn
representatives of certain families lies in the
personal merit of the “founders” of such
families. A lord who is actually noble, as
human nature goes, possibly stands a better
chance of begetting another “ noble lord ” than
does the offspring of a king’s harlot, whose dis-
tinction was founded upon the certainty of his
mother’s lack of virtue, coupled with the
assumption that his father was a royal libertine.
Great mental capacity or decency of char-
acter seldom run in a strain of human
blood, however—often as we find courage
and amiability transmitted from genera-
tion to generation of dogs or horses—and the
system of hereditary nobility is a disgusting
failure the wide world over. Consequently,
Toryism has abandoned all pretence of believing
that there should be any association of titular
distinction with personal merit in the indivi-
dual, and denounces such an idea as absurd.
Whilst The Bulletin thinks that Tennyson,
being a poet, made a miserable mistake in be-
coming a baron, the Argus correspondent con-
siders that the Crown committed a glaring error
in selecting such a man for “honour ” instead,
perhaps, of a scorbutic brewer, a Court flunkey,
or a prominent political failure. Yet even
Toryism hasn’t drawn up a list of quali-
fications for the barontage or peerage,
albeit knighthoods are clearly understood
to be badges of greasy loyalty and
pompous stupidity. Enormous wealth is
not the only guide to hereditary greatness,
although it carries a man well forward towards
a dukedom, especially if he made it out of beer.
Comparatively needy nigger-slayers are pulled
into the House of Lords now and then; a
physician who relieves a royal personage from
the effects of over-feeding is occasionally made
a hart. They join the ranks of the aristocracy
unquestioned, and the brew'er takes them to his
bloated bosom with fervour. It’s all right so
long as they don’t happen to be troubled with
personal merit, in which case they are very
naturally regarded as glaring instances of the
Crown’s absurdity. Australia, fortunately,
need have no fears concerning its nobility. Sir
William Clarke, Baronet, is a guarantee that
hereditary honours are unlikely to be hitched to
personal merit in this hemisphere.

Either an epidemic of dreadfully hard lying
has set in among the survivors of the Emin
Pasha Relief Expedition, or else that gang of
escaped pirates has been distinguishing itself in
quite a number of unexpected ways. Accord-
ing to latest reports Major Barttelot, who
commanded the rearguard, was a homicidal
maniac running amok throughAAfric a gentle-
man who kicked occasional natives to death by
way of exercise, and otherwise conducted him-
self as an affable British explorer upon the loose.
He was haunted by an impression that Stanley
intended to poison all the subordinate officers in
order to monopolise the entire glory, but
whether this idea was based on any sufficient
evidence, and wdiether it drove him mad or
merely arose out of his madness, does not
appear. His assistant, Jamieson, on the other
hand, is said to have been of an artistic turn,
and his speciality was buying little girls for the
cannibals, in order that he might sketch the
victims while they were being killed and cut
up and eaten. This is one side of the story;
the other is that Barttelot and Jamieson
were two honourable gentlemen who were
put out of the way by treachery, lest
they should reveal many shady doings on the
part of the advertising Yankee book-canvasser,
and that the man who rises at this remarkably
late hour to tell a tale which—had it been true
—would almost inevitably have leaked out a
year ago, is ready to swear any atrocious lie that
his arrogant boss prompts him with. In either
case the Stanley expedition is a loathsome
affair, and the one tiling about it which is
beyond all doubt or cavil, is that Stanley’s
claim to have discovered the great forest region
of Africa is a lie, because Du Chaillu was there
before him, but as the French explorer did not
blow his own horn with sufficient vigour,
nobody believed him. Also, Stanley’s dis-
covery of the dwarfs is a fraud, for Du Chaillu
found them years ago, and was set down as a
perjurer for doing it. And, lastly, Stanley’s
story of his awful running fight with the
ferocious men of the woods is either an impos-
ture or a standing proof of the bloodthirsty
character of his expedition, for Du Chaillu
visited the same tribes alone, and was allowed
to pass everywhere unharmed. Most of the
other facts connected with this ghastly show
may be loose and uncertain, but these three are
beyond question.

Surely the principles of ordinary law should
apply to charges against members of Parliament.
The Bulletin contends that if a member
in a state of obvious irresponsibility,
from whatever cause arising, enters the House
and makes a disturbance, his seat should
not be declared vacant forthwith. He should
merely be removed from the House for a term,
say one, two or three days, and then called upon
to say why he should not be finally expelled.
It is all very well to say that such offences
should be punished instantly, that recalcitrant
members should “be made examples of” at
once, and so on ; but if the House’s collective
sense of the wickedness of the offending man is
so weak as to be incapable of surviving a day or
two’s reflection, then it is idle to talk of the
dignity of Parliament. All the most hideous
and brutal blunders of Australian law have
arisen through a desire to move J udge Lynch
into the chair. When a man is arrested in the
street for being drunk and disorderly, he is
placed in durance and allowed to sober up
before the Bench fines him forty shillings with
the option. It is most illogical and unseemly
to try a man while he is “off his head.”
Sufficient for the day is the evil thereof—it is
quite enough for the time being for the House
to have and exercise the power of locking up a
disorderly member, who, as the penalty of ex-
pulsion carries with it awidely-proclaimed social
disgrace, should at least have the legal privi-
leges of the common or garden drunk.
The Maori prophet Mabuki says that the
Almighty has turned lawyer.
The world is now waiting to know how
many natives Stanley’s expedition ate between
them down in Darkest Africa.
The Story of Cyrus Haley.
Dear Bulletin, —Your contributor re Cyrus
Haley, the incendiary and would-be murderer, is
rather loose in his facts. He is right as to Haley’s
actuating cause, bub the facts of the case are
worthy of correct recapitulation. Haley first
burst upon the Stock (share) Exchange, Auckland,
N.Z., as a sharebroker. lie was a handsome,
dignified man, very gentlemanly in manner and
appearance. When the boom was just on the turn
he took the large room back of the great Exchange
Court in the N.Z. Insurance Co.’s buildings and
opened it with much eclat as a swell dinina: room,
furnishing it, without regard to expense, on credit.
The first night it was opened he burnt it, and
though the grand water-supply of Auckland saved
the building that portion was gutted. Haley vyas
pitied—and paid by the Insurance Cos. His wife
was a clever, showy woman bet a dipsomaniac, and
managing to get “ under the influence ” one night
when she had to sing at the Choral Hall, she was
naturally rebuffed by Judge Fenton, the president,
and cold-shouldered by the local fashionables.
Then Cyrus took his little match-box and burnt
the wooden hall down. It was rebuilt, and he
burnt it down again. He was a persevering man,
was Cyrus ! Between whiles he set fire to the
ship City of Auckland, and she had to be scuttled
to save some of her, her repairs costing almost as
much as anew ship. Then Cyrus went out to the
Pah farm (not after breaking gaol, for he never
broke it but once, his last break) and fired Russell’s
haystacks. The kerosene bond of Arohard and
Brown Mechanics Bay, was his next little Guy
Fawkes blaze; to light which he tore aside a sheet
of the corrugated iron of which it was built, and
so gave clear evidence of incendiarism. Two
nights or so after, without changing his buff-
coloured slippers, in which he had been
seen madly pacing his own verandah, by a
servant next door, whose evidence there anent
went strongly against him subsequently, he
clutched his little gun and went out to Tom
Russell s again. That downy C.M.G. was in
England, but Cyrus was unaware of the fact, and
when he knocked at the French door after mid-
night and young Russell got up and pulled aside
the blind, ho tired at him through the glass,
luckily missing him. Russell and a servant girl
both noted the buff slippers as Haley danced
round the verandah. The police were on the
alert—mad, in fact —and when Haley called on
his friends again a night or two after and fired the
remaining haystacks, word was at once wired to
t hvn from Onehunga and Inspector Broham, then
a magnificent athletic figure, aroused Detective
Jeffrey (of unsavoury memory) and the pair, on
horseback, galloped out to try and meet the
incendiary if possible. Going down Kyber
Pass, Broham turned into the Mountain-road
past Seccombe’s Brewery, and sent Jeffrey round
by Newmarket. Well, round the back of Mount
Eden, in the clear moonlight, Broham saw a man
coming along towards him, who, as soon as he
sighted the horseman, turned off the road over a
stone wall and took to the fields. Broham dashed
up to the spot, dismounted, and started after him.
The inspector’s superior speed soon enabled him
to run the suspect down, when the latter turned
and pulled a revolver, pointing it full in Broham’s
face. Brave as a lion, Broham simply said,
“ What, is it you, Haley? You are my prisoner.”
The only reply was, “ Yes, it is I, Broham,” and
he pulled the trigger. How true it is that the
devil, however long he sticks to his votaries,
always deserts thematacriticalmoment! Thepistol
only clicked, and Broham belted his friend over the
head with his loaded whip, closed with him, and
handcuffed the miscreant. “Just my luck,” said
Haley—“ I must even be arrested by a swell, no
poor devil of a constable will get the kudos.”
His defence was masterly, during a long trial, but
he several times made the fatal mistake of asking
a witness a question beginning with, “When I
was So-and-So,” or “ When you saw me at such-
and-such-aplace.” Pure admissions, as any
lawyer knows. Haley was convicted on absolute
proof and sentenced to life-imprisonment. He
was drafted to Dunedin Gaol, and when he had
done about two years he “ took his hook ” off an
outside job, was chased by a warder into the
streets of the town, and being now in better con-
dition than when young Broham ran him down,
was escaping, when the warder stood, called upon
him to stop, and then' sighting at 100 yards and
being a better shot than most officials, drilled a
hole through the small of Cyrus Haley’s back
with a Snider bullet, and that was the end
of one of the coldest-blooded and most dan-
gerous criminals of modern colonial days.
I may add that what saved the life of
Haley’s captor, Broham, and undid Mr. Cyrus,
was the fact that while proceeding up
Syraond-street, on his incendiary errand, Haley
evidently began nursing his gun in gleeful antici-
pation, and, in putting it back into his pocket,
must have disturbed the gear, for the pistol taken
from him by Broham was minus its chambers, and
these were found, all loaded, by a milkman as he
went via Symond-street to town in the dawn
succeeding the eventful night.— Kaitangata.
In Naples the Government has burst up
the Convent of the Buried Alive, an institution
which was detested even by many of the en-
lightened priesthood for its revolting extremes.
The place has existed as a pestilential exaggera-
tion of the worst fanaticism for three centuries.
When the police burst in at last they found 10
nuns, all but insane, existing among incredible
filth and degradation.
Sir Soffcgoods Sargood, who still—by force
of habit, carries a pair of scissors in his waistcoat-
pocket—is the new Victorian Minister of Educa-
tion. Unorßiographic Colonel Smith, who had
the front to expect the portfolio, has been turned
out for a spell. Well do we remember Smith’s
former term of office as Education-Minister.
Smith, at the Black Wednesday time, succeeded
Ramsay as Minister. Ramsay was an attorney
and an educated man. Smith was a mining-
speculator and volunteer demigod, but though he
could give a sweet (strictly official) smile at a
pretty lady teacher, so far as scholastic affairs
went he didn’t know enough to come in out of the
rain. At that time the man who scribbles this was
a humble clerk in the Education-office, and when
he found Smith “sacking” right and left
clever and cultivated men, such as Inspector-
General Wilson Brown (now secretary) and
W. H. Handheld (nßw, as then, chief clerk)
as a reward for their long and faithful services, and
installing in their places a set of his own friends,
whose official English would disgrace even
Parliament, he rebelled at the monstrous job
and lay in wait for the incoming gang. Firstly,
it was’ discovered that Minister of Education
Smith was in the habit of spelling “until” with
two ‘ I’s ” and “recommend” with one “in” in
official minutes, and when that got into the papers
by some mysterious means there was much trouble
among the Smith crowd. Finally, the office fell
into such hopeless confusion that nearly all the
efficient men dismissed had to be called back.

Thus Sir William Robinson, at the Mel-
bourne Mayor’s banquet:—“ I stand amongst
friends who have not forgotten that for a few
months —too few so far as I was concerned—l
worked with them as a fellow citizen in the
endeavour to advance the best interests of the
colonji.” No, Sir William. The democracy can-
not recognise the fellow-citizenship of a nominee
of an outside power, which is farcically called that
democracy’s “Sovereign Lady.” A Governor,
who is in no sense elected by, or representative of,
the people, is, and can be, “ the fellow-citizen ”of
no one. He is a hireling marionette, a phono-
graph-automaton, a speaking doll, and, as often as
not, a Flunkey of the Flunkeys. How would you
take it if we claimed to bo your fellow-Governor ?

the N.S.W. Chairman of Committees, is unduly

Barton.
One of the pleasantest reflections awakened
by the fall of the Gillies Cabinet is'that-—for a
time, at least —the soft, persuasive voice of Duncan
will no longer be heard promising to “niak’ a
note of it.” Gillies never refused to do anything,
or to be anything, or to give anything under the
sun ; he merely rose to his feet in a fat, benevolent
way and said that “if the hon. gentleman would
withdraw his amendment for the present, the
Government would mak’a note o’ the circumstance
and would give it their immediate consideration.”
If the other man was obstinate he would offer to
“make a careful note of it,” and generally his
opponent gave in. But whether the old political
smiler made a note, or made a careful note, the
result was exactly the same —in either case he
merely wrote down the item in fugitive ink on the
empty air, and no one ever taw or heard of it
again. The scheme worked very well for several
years, but at last the other members of the Cabinet
got into the habit of saying that they' also would
“ male’ a note of it,” and then the House resolved
that it would stand the melancholy chestnut no
longer.
Sir William Robinson, the rele'gue Gover-
nor of West Australia, has a palpitating yearning
for the fleshpots of Melbourne. The few months
during which he ivigned there, after Loch and
before Hopetoun, glisten in his memory like the
pollen on the thighs of a bee backing out of a full-
bloomed flower-cup. He made a big and unblu-h
ing bid for the reversion of Hopetomi’s billet by
saying in public last week that when Federation
came along Melbourne would most likely be the
seat of central government. In other vtords, lie
saw fit to heave into the market-place the burning
faggots o i jealousy. The very same fear and sus-
picion that have kept back Northern Queensland
from moving as a whole for Territorial Separation
from Southern Queensland, have operated to retard
Australian Federation. Every other N.Q. town
has been afraid that the Separation move was
merely a Townsville “ boom,” as Townsville made
no secret of its pretensions to be the capital of the
new colony. So, in the matter of Australian
Federation, the hitches of the past can be clearly
traced to the suspicion that the Melbourne
“ boomers,” or the Sydney “ boomers,” were try-
ing to “work the oracle.”
Dear Bulletin, — Gov. Hopetoun hit the
nail on the head during a recent speech at Mel-
bourne, wherein he suggested that the money and
labour spent in missionising the blacks and
Chinese would be put to a better use in raising
and educating the youth of our own slums. If
there is one task more hopeless than all others
in this world it is the task of converting a
blackfellow. His soul—if he has a soul—never
rises higher than rum and mutton, and as to
getting him to grasp the doctrine of the Trinity,
or even the Atonement —well, it’s simply an im-
possibility. The average aboriginal is utterly
unable to understand the simple matter of
principle. The blackfellow who has been in the
“Mission” for years will lie, swear, get drunk
and steal with as great a gusto as his untutored
brother. The lapses of the model couple held up
to the public some time ago by the enterprising
Mr. Daniel Matthews, of Maloga, N.S.W., are
known to everyone, and furnish an illustration (o
the above contention. The experience of the late
Mrs, Robertson, of Hexham* (Vic.), is also
greatly to the point. This well-meaning lady
took two blacks, male and female, and arrayed
them in orthodox clothing. She then taught
them to write, &c., giving the boy various handy
accomplishments, and making the girl proficient
in sewing and knitting. But what was the
result ? Whenever a tribe came near the home-
stead (it was thirty years ago), off went the pair,
their clothes were soon cast aside and traded off
for rum, and an old blanket completed the
transformation. They were often brought back,
but with the same result. At last the sorely-
harassed lady had to abandon the job, aha to
admit reluctantly that the “material wasn’t
there.” “As it was then so it is now,” and ever
will be until we see the last of the last nigger,-

840 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Nov. If), 1890,
My Dear Moorabinda, —
I wonder if the high Victorian marriage-rate, of which
we have lately heard so much, is due to the existence of
that flourishing Melbourne matrimonial agency where
for two-and-six you can be introduced to eligibility in the
parlour across the hall? In the interests of The Bulletin
your Sappho was ushered into the parlour, the other day.
It was a small, square room, dark even in the morning,
crammed with soft furniture and floored with noiseless
velvety carpet. Portieres shrouded the door—there was
a sofa, of course, and a piano at which the victims (you
fill in what are your accomplishments in the schedule of
application) go through their musical paces during the
awful interviews. The gas-globes were pink—a nude
pink-coloured “ love ” hung from the gasalier, the air was
thick with a sweet sickly scent, and—was it altogether
chance that the pictures on the walls were mostly lifelike
studies from thenude? In the stuffy, languorous atmosphere
the lady who does the introducing—a small woman with
a black fringe on her forehead and heavily scented—con-
sented to explain things. Doctors, lawyers and clergy-
men (there wex - e many on her books then) infested the
agency, and she was simply a society match-maker on a
large scale. Of course anybody could apply if they
wanted to, but the agency was quite a toney establish-
ment. Of course only two people are allowed to meet at
at time, so there were five parlours in case of a boom,
“ all furnished like this ” with a wave of a dingy palm to
the pictures, the sofa, the piano. “ People laxurh at
agencies, but we do an immense serious business all over
Austi'alia.” She told me that more women apply for
husbands, than men for wives, and the heiresses invari-
ably scoop up a Darby—for half-a-orown. But the
gentlemen pay a commission according to the bride’s
income, the lovely woman without coin costing little
over the preliminary two-and-six, while the bill “ to
supplying one heiress ” comes in regularly with the gas-
account for over a year. She gave me a schedule in
which you filled in more or less accurate particulars as to
“age” (!), “personal appearance,” “general health,”
“ temper,” &o. (it reminded me of a horse-dealer’s cata-
logue), and I blinked my way out. In the hall a grimy
child was swinging on the front door, the finger-panels of
which were grease-mottled, and there was a faint smell of
onions warring with the cloud of patchouli that burst
with me out of the parlour. “ The very beet people are
our clients,” said the lady, “and you see our simple
method is the same as ladies meeting gentlemen in their
own drawing-rooms. I could show you dozens of letters
thanking us over and over again for the happiness we
have brought to the writers, and you would be surprised
at the signatures.” “ Do these letters come during the
honeymoon?” asked yours ever, innocently. “Mostly
during the honeymoon,” blandly replied the lady.
Melbourne Austral Salon has changed its lair from
Swanston-sti-eet to the skylight of the Austral Buildings,
Collins-street, at the corner of Punch (which now surely
should be called Judy) Lane. The new “stand” consists
of two rooms, and, I am glad to see, a strietly temperance
buffet, though probably the “sinking” member will be
able, if she tries vex - y hard, to surreptitiously import,
without exciting remark, a little Dutch courage to mix
with her ginger-ale ; and the excessively balloonish view
of the Block from the front windows must quite disgust
the member whose charms aren’t built to hit the bull’s-
eye from the top of the house. Bric-k-brac, dust-coloui’ed
linoleum, with the walls of a sad sea-green (when “eternal
fitness ” howled for blue), combined’with doors en suite,
splotched with “ impressionist ” blobs of gold paint, which
looked in a general way as if the peroxide dye-bottle had
been upset, formed the background of the salon, which
shortly adopts the humane idea of having a floating loan
collection of art, when the chromos on the wall will be
changed once a quarter. The Club has now achieved the
dignity of a surplus, and with its two hundred assorted
members, and more coming, its cultured shriek ought to
waft down the breeze for centuries.
.Speaking of yells reminds me that I am sent a song
entitled, “ God Bless our Trades-Unions,” by J. 0. Halbert.
The outpouring is in three-four time as a general prin-
ciple, the chorus altering suddenly to common time, with
the same effect as when you step on a stair that isn’t
there. The melody is inoffensive and unassuming, and
the lines are all rhyme. Could you produce a better
common or garden echo than “docker ”and “ shocker? ”
Lady Galloway, who is to accompany Lady Jersey to
Sydney, is a half-sister of Lord Salisbury’s. She is Lady
Jersey’s great ally, and has promised to see that illus-
trious Primrose Leaguer through at least six months of
her misery.
The reason why Governors’ wives generally turn up
accompanied by a disaffected “mate” who can “pout
and be perverse” without doing any tangible damage to
those silken bonds, and who can breezily snub all the
“impossibles” by proxy for the officially amiable vice-
regal lady, has just been discovered by an English paper.
It says “ Governors of colonies find interest and occupa-
tion in so many different ways that they seldom have
much time for yearning (!) for their home friends; hut
with their wives the case is somewhat different, and some
of them become too obviously home-sick. So they take
out with them a bosom friend or relative to mingle their
tears with, over the vanished ‘ Home, sweet home !’ ” By-
the-by, this vioe-iegal trick of attaching a lady to the
retinue is being copiously copied by the wives of one or
two dignitaries who affect a similar satellite though they
are surrounded by sisters, cousins and aunts, and can
almost throw a stone on the roof of the house where
they were born.
I am told of a zealous inspector on the Sydney Public-
school Board who was recently putting a teacheress over
her jumps. “Can yer sew?” “Oh, yes,” replied the
victim. “But yer don’t so much,” said the inquisitor,
catching her white and shapely left hand, “ because yer
finger aint pricked.”
The Hon. Rupert Carington is still prowling round
Melbourne in his novel capacity of bridegroom-elect. It
is whispered that the wedding-day is really fixed for a
date in December.
With a view to preserving the dignity of Sydney women
who may visit Melbourne in ignorance of the following
facts, your Sappho wishes to point out that the social
aspect of the convenient hansom cab differs widely in
George-street and Collins-street. Fortunately for the
Sydneyite who doesn’t own a vehicle, the hansom cab is,
in the city of the Beautiful Harbour, a recognised and
proper conveyance only to be looked at through your
lingers when ail the blinds are down, and into which
(when the blinds are up) the tired, returning shopkeeper
or the woman who wants to go where a tram can’t take
her, can step without any impropriety. But in Melbourne
the cab is regarded in a very giddy light, and the woman
who hails one from the Block is suspected of having dyed
hair and fourteen husbands and oyster-supper proclivities;
so the girl with an eye to the Cabbage-plot conveyances
must either walk or hire a hideous, comfortless wag-
gonette. In Brisbane, also, hansoms are regarded from the
Melbourne standpoint.
A quiet, simple ceremony was the wedding of Miss
Read, daughter of the late Reginald Bligh Bead, and
granddaughter of the late Hon. Edward Flood, with Mr.
“ Sam ” Baylis, of Sydney, on 4th inst. It was a house-
wedding and took place at the “ Firs,” Coogee, the resi-
dence of the bride’s mother. The principal lady wore a
grey silk gown, relieved with forget-me-nots, and was
shrouded in a real Maltese lace veil in which her mother
also had been married. She had two bridesmaids, the
Misses Peter.

while the gentlemen are requested to roll up as bears.
The hugging will come quite natural to them, I am sure.

A rather cruel incident in connection with the depar-
ture of the Caringtons from Redfern Station was that the
crowd caught sight of the cook on the vice-regal train
and shouted “ Goodbye, cookie !” And as the cortege
moved slowly off, Lord Carington was waving his hat at
one window, while the cook agitated his white cap at
another !! What a complete and perfect thing the spec-
tacle of that cook waving his calico-topping at the crowd
made of the vice-regal departure, didn’t it?
Legal officials become so suspicious and sceptical that
they wouldn’t believe their grandmothers on oath. A
Victorian sheriff, engaged in an ejectment case the other
day, was about to take the roof off the house and knock
in the windows, when he was told that the proceedings
must be stayed, as the wife of the tenant whose rent was
invisible, had just augmented the family by one. The
mere miserable statement satisfied everybody but the
sheriff, who smiled a cautious smile and ordered the baby
to be brought to him, and when a small, pink atomy was
hurriedly produced, he wasn’t quite satisfied, but pinched
the infant to see if it was real!

I wonder how much lower and looser evening bodices
will scoop dorsally? Certainly we don’t have to scoff
at so much ddoolLt6 in high places but the prevailing
loose, square back with its flounces of frilled chiffon,
inside which the wearer looks like a small kernel in a
large nut, is a very Irish improvement on the V-shaped
worry. By the way, sleeveless evening bodices are now
hopelessly “ out,” and no matter how well-moulded your
arm may be, you must shroud it from shoulder to knuckles
in something—a tight fit in fisher-net is the best, if yor
pride yourself on the dimple in your elbow.
What might be called the “convict crop” is a new
Melbourne fashion for arranging your front hair, visible
in large quantities at Plemington with various effects. It-
is a straight fringe cut squarely and pugilistically across-
the wearer’s brow—uncurled, unfrizzed—simply oiled and.
brushed down in a geometrical line from temple to temple.
It looks too hideously historical to be permanently
adopted by the very best people.

send ss. in stamps to Box 0., G.P.O. when the photo-
graph would be forwarded, &c., &c. At least several
victims fell into the snare set for their vanity.

Koch and Phthisis.
The cables say that a toxalbumen is the chief in-
gredient in Koch’s new cure for consumption, and
people who believe the cables are much concerned
to know what toxalbumen may be. Hr. Ashburton
Thompson, who is a likely man to know something
about it, says that it is a product thrown off by
the bacteria, which is a deadly poison to them-
selves. It is a queer thing that life can only be
kept going by the incessant manufacture of things
that are fatal to life. A human being passes his
whole life in turning good fresh air into a poisonous
vapour called carbonic acid gas, which doesn’t kill
him off once a minute or so only because
it is constantly being diluted by fresh supplies
of oxygen. If pure carbonic acid gas were
turned into a roomfull of people, it would simply
wipe them out; and Dr. Thompson suggests that
Koch’s plan may be to turn the pure toxalbumen
into a lung full of bacteria. The scheme has a
plausible look about it, and there doesn’t seem to
be any reason why it shouldn’t do for consumption
just as effectually as Tenner’s vaccination did for
smallpox or Pasteur’s attenuated breeds, of bacilli
for the silkworm-disease or the cattle-plague.
Koch’s main difficulty has been to kill the disease-
germ without killing the patient at the same time.
It was discovered not long ago that sunlight was
fatal to the thing, but hospital patients seemed
prejudiced against iiaving their lungs taken out
and hung up in the back yard for ten minutes, so
the idea had to be given up. If there is anything
in the toxalbumen theory—as there most likely is
not, seeing it comes by cable —it seems to start the
cure of germ-diseases on a new line. The old
theory of inoculation was a different one. Pro-
fessor Tyndall explained it something after this
fashion ; If you burn down a big tree, you find as
a residue a very small quantity of white
ashes. That represents the mineral matter
that entered into the constitution of the
tree. There isn’t much of it, but such as
it is it was necessary, and if it had not
been present in the soil the tree could not have
grown there. Now, suppose that before the tree
started, some other plant had exhausted all that
particular element in the ground thereabouts, ob-
viously the tree could never have lived. In the
same way the germ of a disease can only take root
and flourish in the body if it finds there the par-
ticular elements it requires for its support; and if
we keep our body clear of those elements we can
defy the disease. For example, according to this
theory, smallpox can only thrive where it finds a
special sort of nourishment in the blood. Introduce a
little cow-pox by vaccination, and it travels through
the system in search of this very nourishment,
and when the stock is exhausted it dies. Let a
small-pox germ come along after that and it can’t
possibly take root. There is not a particle of food
left for it in the body, and it is killed by sheer
starvation without having power to raise a solitary
pustule. David Walker might as well try to take
up a collection in the Y.M.O.A. rooms the day
after the Melbourne Cup.
The Maori Pig Market.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In distant New Zealand, whose tresses of gold
The billows are ceaselessly combing,
Away in a village all tranquil and old
I came on a market where porkers were sold —
A market for pigs in the gloaming.
And Maoris in plenty in picturesque rig
The lands of their forefathers roaming,
Were weighing their swine, whether little or big,
For purchasers paid by the weight of the pig—
The weight of the pig in the gloaming.
And one mighty chieftain, I grieve to relate,
The while that his porker was foaming
And squealing like fifty—that Maori sedate,
He leant on the pig just to add to its weight—
He leant on the pig in the gloaming.
Alas ! For the buyer, an Irishman stout—
O’Grady, I think, his cognomen—
Perceived all his doings, and, giving a shout,
With the butt of his whip laid him carefully out
By the side of his pig in the gloaming.
A terrible scrimmage did straightway begin,
And I thought it was time to be homing,
For Maoris and Irish were fighting like sin
’Midst war-cries of “Pakeha !” “ Batherashin !”
While the pigsdid their best to lend tone to the din
i As I fled from the spot in the gloaming.

Mr, Jenkins, of Buckingbong Station, has
notified the shearers who struck work and had
their earnings judicially forfeited that he will now
pay them all the money in question, some £l5O.
Says the good Sydney clergyman to the
seventy-year-old man : “Here, my friend, go and
work on my land. Your screw will be ten
bob a week, payable in rations, and be sure and
don’t make a beast of yourself. ”
The son of a Melbourne comedian married
•against his father’s wish. However, he daringly
took his bride right to his father’s door and
knocked. The old man came himself. Says the
son: “Father, we’ve come.” The comedian
called upstairs to his wife: “Mary, they’ve
come!” Then he added: “Now, you may go
away again.”
Mr. Perrott, the highly-respected P.M. of
Wallsend and Waratah, had a somewhat novel
experience the other day. The P.M. owns a cow.
That cow strayed, and at the instance of the
Inspector for the Borough Council the P.M. had
to issue a summons against himself as the owner
of the animal. The P.M. sat alone on his own
case, and fined himself one solitary bob ! Fact-

Although staged with the cretonne-
covered furniture of the cheap Chinese period
half-rehearsed, incorrectly-costumed and not so
strongly cast as on former occasions in Sydney
“She Stoops to Conquer” pleased Royal audiences
satiated with love and rant. Bellew’s Youne-
Marlow was a good performance except when he
attempted to simulate a modesty that was
altogether foreign to his nature, when the effort
was too palpable. Mrs. Potter’s monotonous
delivery, occasionally coarse and wooden enuncia-
tion, were much in evidence as Kate Hardcastle
In her second costume —an atrocity in red and
green, with a straw-scoop full of flowers for hat
which she declares to be chronologically correct—
she resembled a bon-bon or a French doll at a fancy
fair. In her arch scenes she developed to an alarm-
ing extent her lack of repose. Each point was em-
phasised with a jerky bob of the head, every sen-
tence was punctuated with a nod at every comma
and a sweeping bow or curtsey for a full stop.
That kind of thing makes folks tired. The Tony
of Mr. Poulton was a nimble-heeled, oily-tongued
amusing person who savoured more of the Strand
than of Goose-lane. Lumpkin, as his name implies
is a booby, whose tongue is tipped with the dialect
of his alehouse.companions, and who is somewhat
heavy-footed with trudging over clay pastures
though by no means an absolute (down. Edvvin
Palmer’s Hardcastle was a creditable performance,

With but one thinly-drawn-out thread of
story, no underplot, and only one situation of
consequence, “Hero and Leander ” chiefly com-
mends itself as a very pretty spectacle and a lime-
lit study of the nearly-nude. The lines, bar an
occasional assonance, such as “Here lies the lyre,”
are fairly good—albeit some of them sound like
close paraphrases of stronger stuff, and there is
not a sentence in the whole production that
compels admiration and takes memory by storm.
Hennings’ scene in the court of the temple of
Aphrodite is very beautiful and received quite an
ovation on the premiere. No one seemed greatly
taken with the Greek dance : borrowed, it is
said, from “ Claudian,” its music is Moorish
in character, and its movements Caledonian.
So far, audiences have been largely composed of
ladies, who come in most cases laden with flowers
to cover up, like the robins, these babes who are so
destitute of decent clothing. This preponderance
of the softer sex is probably due to the fact that
although more of the fair Cora is seen than we
ever hoped or expected, Ivyrle Bellew is even less
reticent. So little can be said either in blame or
praise of these favourites of fortune, that the
dailies were compelled to speak rapturously and at
length of the lady’s “marvellous leap of at least
15 feet ” into the Hellespont, though why the bag
of sawdust that received her fairy “ sixes ” escaped
favourable mention, we know not. On the con-
clusion of the Sydney season, the gentle Twinning
leads the gushing pair up to Newcastle for two
nights, 7th and Bth December; thence, probably,
to New Zealand, and on return perchance to Bris-
bane. As the lady has a firm foothold on Austra-
lian soil we may yet see more of hex - , perhaps in
another house.

Harry Rickards’ sixteenth week at Sydney
School of Arts was, financially, the most success-
ful of a very successful season. On Monday
night, assisted by Lew Britton and many other
amateurs, he benefited, when the house slopped
over into the corridors all round, and much money
was turned away. Opening on Saturday at Bris-
bane Royal for four weeks, Mr. Rickards returns
to St. George’s Hall, Melbourne, on Boxing-night,
then pays Sydney allying visit ere going to Britain
for a new company.

The starving man of Bondi, advised by the
doctors to follow the flesh on account of the injury
that abstinence may do his constitution, having-
lost twenty-eight pounds in twenty-one days, still
continues to work up a fine appetite for Christmas.
As yet, however, Jacques, who has a lean and
lxungry look, hasn’t received a single invite, and
some unfortunate hash-house is bound to go bung
when he breaks oxxt on the eat. The wily Wyburd,
wlxo is now lessee of the aquarium, has given the
entire use of it for one day to the Relief Fund.
* *
Five-doctors the other day . certified that
the physical condition of Ibbott was such that
should he continue his 40 days’ fast at Bondi
aquarium they would in no way be responsible tor
the consequences. Nevertheless, Mr. Ibbott
elected to continue. Is it not now the duty ol
the Crown to send its own medical men to inspect
Mr Ibbott, and, should the opinion of the latter
doctors agree with that of the others, to have
Ibbott arrested for attempting to commit suicide,
and to feed him artificially, if necessary ?

Many ladies admire grey hair—on some other
person—but few care to try its effects on their
own charms. They need not, since Ayer’s Hair
Vigor restores grey hair to its original color.
Sold by druggists and perfumers.
Dumas’ play, “ The Clemenceau Case,”
having failed at first to draw at New York Stan-
dard, the management engaged Sybil Johnstone
to play an artist’s model. She was discovered,
when the curtain rolled up, standing on a pedestal
in the centre of the stage, her arms uplifted above
her head, her figure clad only in a silk jersey and
silken tights, without corset, and without trunks.
The silk was of flesh-colour, and fitted glove-like
over every swelling and hollow of her person.
She is a young woman, rather pretty, and with a
form of voluptuous beauty. The frank revelation
of her graces of contour stunned the audience.
The police authorities were notified next day, but,
according to latest reports, they have not com-
pelled the actress to put on more clothes. Women
will not visit the theatre, but it is crowded every
night with men. “ The Clemenceau Case ’’would
be a good thing to put on after “ Hero and
Leander” have run themselves dry at Sydney
Royal. By the way, contrary to the custom at
New York, more women than men have rushed to
see “Hero and Leander.” Possibly, because
Leander is even less attired than Hero.
* m *
Someone, a Sydney alderman, writes to a
Sydney paper complaining of the cruelty which
“ parades for revenue purposes on our streets that
dumb, but noble, animal,” the Cyclorama horse,
with his coat of many colours. Now, we want to
protest against the cruelty which makes the old
gent, dye his moustache, and the middle-aged
husband-hunter fill up her wrinkles with white-
wash and red-raddle. The dyeing of a horse, it is
true, constitutes an indecent offence against a
noble animal, but we presume that so long as non-
poisonous dyes, not interfering with the moke’s
skin-action, are used, it will be considered that
the offence is merely one against the public eye
and the eyes of other horses.

** * *
The Dampier - Walch derangement of
“Robbery Under Arms” was viewed at Mel-
bourne Alexandra on Saturday night, when a
vast multitude rocked the cradle of Australian
national drama with their feet, and the native
gallery-boy whistled and howled like the wind
amongst the gums of the primeval forest. Enthusi-
asm ever runs high at the Dampierian theatre, but
on these special occasions of the reigning family’s
return from a provincial tour, public feeling
appears to slop over and drown a good deal of the
dialogue. If the Australian drama possesses a
fault it is in being too exciting for pale, con-
templative people. “ Robbery "Under Arms,”
taken in conjunction with its general reception,
resembles a deep draught of bush rum, and a
gaudy evening with Starlight and his gang is apt
to be followed by an aching brow. Certainly no
other theatre on the continent can offer such a
succession of earthquakes for the money. Satur-
day’s performance seemed none the worse for a few
changes from the original cast. The old people, too,
have improved in their parts, with the exception
of Miss Katherine Russell, who expresses her
love, jealousy and remorse as though she were
thinking about something quite different at the
time. There is evidently plenty of profit left in
“Robbery Under Arms,” the first exhaustive
run notwithstanding. And this for the reason
that the drama; besides being genuinely Aus-
tralian in itself, is interpreted in an Australian
spirit and put upon the stage with an affectionate
regard for the land of its birth. Lily Dampier’s
riding-habit is a trifle out of harmony with her
bush life, all the same. Dampier here might
write in a tender passage explaining that Starlight
had ordered the garment from town, as a birth-
day-present for the cattle-shaker’s daughter, or
something of that sort. Which wouldn’t account
for such a fascinating fit, anyway.

Other Melbourne shows remain as before.
“Impulse ’ is doing good business at the Bijou,
and sassiety evidently approves of the amorous
Frenchman's discomfiture in the pursuit of his
prey. Judged by the rules of advanced civilisa-
tion, this stab of illicit affection is decidedly out
of luck, a fact which the author tacitly admits by
allowing him to depart in peace. The same
character in substantial drama would inevitably
find himself dropped down a well, or otherwise
sacrificed to the fury of Mrs. Grundy, but in this
instance theymerelyshowhimthedoorlike sensible
Christians and recommend him to let his guilty
passion cool down in foreign parts. Miss Romer’s
share in the. Impulse” success was overlooked
last week. She is lively as usual in the capacity
of a widow who takes notes by the way of every-
thing that threatens spicy disclosures, and goes
around boring through the privacy of domestic
life with a gimlet Her references to the poor
young spinster who must get married are shock-
ingly appetising.

** * *
The Victoria Hall keeps going, and the
Gaiety is to be re-opened by Frank Clark on
Saturday. An amateur performance of “Chil-
penc,’ at the Opera House, was of a maddening
character, but Harmston’s Circus is all right for
such as hunger after such things. The Museum
has got hold of something which looks nice on the
posters outside, and Madame Patey’s efforts to fill
the Town Hall are meeting with better success
than they did at first. But it is hard to make the
new-chum portion of the public perceive the force
°f paying five shillings to hear Madame Patey,
and very little else. More especially as this
excellent artist’s voice doesn’t sound a day younger
than it was 15 years ago.
** * *
“ Chums,” by David Christie Murray, was
recently produced, for the first time in Australia,
at Adelaide Royal, where the attendances were
not up to the excellence of the performance. The
scene of the play is laid in New Zealand, where
the piece was written and first performed. It has
some improbable situations (what piece lacks
them ?), biit considered as a first attempt at
dramatisation for the Australasian stage, the result
is fairly satisfactory. The characters are true to
life, and well distributed. There is a squatter,
well represented by Fred. Neebe; his station-
manager, skilfully played by Harry St. Maur; a
private enquiry-agent of Scotch extraction, excel-
lently pourtrayed by George Carey, and the
villain, an unscrupulous land-agent, well played
by Christie Murray himself. The principal
attraction, however, is achild-part, which is gripped
by a remarkably clever boy known as Little Leo,
who was the original Bootle’s Baby in Melbourne.
This surprising youngster, the son of one of the
lady members of the company (professionally
known as Eily Mayo), will, it is understood,
shortly attempt Little Lord Fauntleroy. Florence
Seymour plays naturally in a suitable part, and
the other members of the company worked with a
will to make matters “go.” The moral mind of
the Adelaideps is now agitated by “ Betsy ”
and a hint as to the future production “Pink
Dominoes.”
** * *
“ Uncle Tom ” Lewis stated that any
amount of kissing went on behind the scenes at
Melbourne Opera House—but then there was so
much hymn-singing and prayer to Heaven in
John Widow O’Brien Sheridan’s show. Now that
the theatre is devoted to comic opera (under Mus-
grove’s management) the position of affairs is
changed, and whereas some members of the
former company may have prayed on the stage and
kissed at the back, the present one hugs on the
stage and prays “ off.” A book upon the spiritual
influence of tights has yet to be written,
*» * *
The news that Sara Bernhardt’s son (whose
illegitimacy makes him painfully sensitive on the
subject of his ma, no doubt) has seriously wounded
one of her newspaper critics in a duel, reminds us
that Bulletin notices are occasionally received in
an un-Christian spirit by showmen who would
prefer to write the aforesaid notices themselves.
Not so very long ago The Bulletin ordered a
first-class funeral for one of the staff, on the under-
standing that he was either to be killed in a duel
or murdered from the rear or sat upon until he
was no more by a midget comedian who cannot
appreciate the beautiful truth when he sees it set
up in type. No corpse came to hand, however, so
the hearse was sent empty away, and the chief
mourners went silently home to tea. After all,
the unslain seems tolerably likely to die a
natural death, because a critic of that description
wears the breastplate of righteousness and can
usually aim straight with a brick. But rather
than waste a funeral, The Bulletin will gladly
bury one or more of the enemy, or as much of them
as is left undevoured by the object of their ven-
geance after they have fired low and been laid out.

One of the chief attractions in the “ Gon-
doliers ” to the bald-headed is the fact that, as the
company is almost continually dancing in a crowd,
one or more of the ladies comes to grief nightly
and sheds her clothing about the stage. Ida
Osborne, who is eclipsing her “Yeoman of the
Guard” performance, flings her shoes recklessly
towards the flies while Eyley waits for a catch at
point or leg and fixes them on again, oh, so nicely.
Little Graupner has a weakness for getting her
skirts torn off in the cachuca—-a dance in which
each female kicks out wildly, springs two feet in
the air and falls on one knee, and then does the
same thing over again. Both Ida and Flora are
built for the cachuca.

characteristic anecdote of Salvini. Some years ago
his wife died, and he erected a handsome monument to
her memory, upon which was a tablet setting forth the
various virtues and graces of the deceased, and recording
the grief of the bereaved husband. When he married his
second wife, he defaced and mutilated the monument of
wife number one with his own hands, berating himself the
while as a fool for expressing such sentiments.

Says Agnes Robertson Boucicault: “I have come, not
forthe property of Mr. Boucicault, but to vindicate my
children and myself. I will remain here until the children
aie protected by the American courts, as they have been
by English tribunals.” Asked if it was probable that an
amicable settlement would be reached with Mrs. Thorn-
dyke Boucicault, she replied, “In the matter of honour,
there will be no compromise. It is not a question of
money. Igo back to England as soon as the decision is
reached in the courts.”
An alligator, trapped at Normanton, is being shown in
Sydney. He was brought down in a wooden box 20 feet
long, 4 feet wide, and about 5 feet deep, barred on top
with iron. The saurian is 17 feet long, and 30 inches in
width. His jaws are 2ft. 4in. long, and his mouth con-
tains 64 teeth in first-class preservation. The gentleman
will be requisitionised to stand for Parliament at next
general election.
