A lot of stupid people who had been to see the Ober-
\mmergau “Passion Play” wrote to the British papers
to expose the blasphemy of those who shake hands with
the principal character after the show is over. They say
rothing, however, of the audacious man who threw a
bouquet and sent a pink note to the Virgin.
Says an American paper;—Mrs. Potter’s attempt at
Cleopatra was a piece of dramatic impertinence. She
used her own incompetency, her inability to do more than
burlesque the master, as an advertisement. The
great Queen was pourtrayed in five Worth cos-
tumes of great magnificence, and the delicate
points of the character were given by a shook of
auburn hair and a pair of scraggy arms. Shakespeare
was insulted and burlesqued, and the actress, unlike
many of her ilk who revel on to the end in blissful ignor-
ance, was party to the trick. And yet everyone went to
see her, and they all liked it, and laughed, and came home
quite pleased with the show, which, it was generally con-
ceded, was a success financially and sensationally. ' Mrs.
Potter was a good drawing card, and she drew better in
Cleopatra because the whole thing was so funny and
incongruous.

It is now said that Dion Boucicault’s real name was
Dionysius Boursiquot. His front name, of course, was
given him in compliment to his godfather, Dr. Lardner.
His father, a nice old gentleman who had married a pretty
Irish girl much younger than himself, kept a draper’s
shop in Palace Yard, off Dame-street, Dublin. He was a
Frenchman who had emigrated on account of religious or
political persecution, or some other of those reasons that
foreigners give to account for their presence in English-
speaking countries.

Sensational-boy-yarn Brett has one of the finest collec-
tions of old armour in Britain. He recently imported
£5OOO worth personally purchased in Russia. Hence his
acquaintance with Irving, and presence among the literati
on the first night of “ Ravenswood.” His armour should
come in handy on future first nights.
Pauline Lucca is about to abandon the stage for teach-
ing. Years ago Bismarck was one of her most devoted
admirers, and on one occasion a profound sensation was
created in Germany by the appearance in the shop-
windows of a photograph in which the Chancellor stood
side by side with the diva. He had, in a frisky moment
stood with her before the lens-of course, without any
idea that the photograph would be made public. Bis-
naarck wrote a letter to the Emperor, in which he ex-
plained the circumstances of a harmless little adventure.
The letter, like the photograph, was published, and added
greatly to the vogue of Pauline Lucca.
Lady Dunlo is playing Venus at the Grand, at Islington.
Says a London writer; “Of her beauty it is hardly neces-
sary to speak, for all the world remembers Belle Bilton ;
but what struck me very forcibly is that Lady Dunlo, for
a new comer to the regular stage, possesses an elegance
and ease of manner, a piquancy in giving the cue, and a
general sayoir fairs which is highly promising indeed. Of
all the ladies who have travelled to the theatrical stage
via_ Queen’s Bench Junction, Lady Dunlo is likely to
arrive first.” It is so easy to discern talent when it is
titled.
George Moore had a play in hand at the Paris Odeon
while an adaption of “ Othello” was in active rehearsal.
Calling one day to see the manager: “ What name shall I
give, monsieur? ” demanded the concibrge. “XellM.
Porell that the English author whose play he has accepted
desires to see him. The concibrge went toward the
manager’s room. “There is a gentleman in the hall who
tells me he is the English author whose play has just been
accepted,” he said to the official. “ Quite right,”
answered the latter; “send him in. Monsieur Shake-
speare, no doubt.”

LOTION
His Wife’s Jewels.
M. Lautin was a head clerk in the employ o f
th Minister of the Interior.
O ne evening he attended a soiree at the house
of his vice-principal, and there met a young girl,
with whom he fell desperately in love at first
sigh* s - , . .
Her father, a country physician, had died some
months before, and soon after she and her mother
took up their abode in Paris, where they visited
frequently among their acquaintances, madame
hoping thereby to secure a favorable parti for her
daughter. They were poor and honorable, quiet
and unassuming in their manners.
The young girl was an admirable type of the
virtuous woman, to whom every sensible young
man dreams of one day confiding his heart and
happiness.
Her simple beauty had the charm of angelic
modesty, and the heavenly smile which con-
stantly hovered about her lips seemed to be the
reflection of a pure and lovely soul.
Her praises resounded on every side. People
never tired of repeating: “Happy the man who
wins her love ! He could not find a better wife.”
Now, M. Lautin enjoyed a little income of
three thousand five hundred francs, and thinking
he could assume the resuonsibilities of a husband,
proposed to this model young girl and was
accepted. ...
He was unspeakably happy with her; she
governed his household with an economy so clever
that they seemed to live in luxury. She lavished
the most delicate attentions on her . husband,
coaxed and fondled him; and the charm of her
presence was so great that six years after his mar-
riage M. Lautin discovered that he loved his wife
even far more than during the first days of their
honeymoon.
He only felt inclined to blame in her two faults :
her love of the theatre
and a taste for falsi
jewellery.
Her friends (she was
acquainted ' with some
officers’wives) frequently
procured for her a box
at the theatre, often for
the first representations
of every new play ; and
her husband was obliged
to accompany her,
whether he willed Or not,
to these amusements,
which bored him exces-
sively after his day’s
labour at the office.
After a time, M, Lautin
begged his wife to request
some lady of her acquain-
tance to accompany her.
She was at first op-
posed to such an ar-
rangement, but after
much persuasion on his
part, she finally con-
sented, to the infinite
delight of her husband.
Now, with her love for
the theatre came also the
desire to decorate her
person. True, her cos-
tumes remained as be c oro
—simple and of the most
correct taste; but she
soon began to adorn her
ears with huge Rhine-
stones, which glittered
and sparkled like rani
diamonds. Around In r
neck she wore strings < f
false pearls, and, on her
arms bracelets of imita-
tion gold.
Herhusband frequently
remonstrated with her,
saying :
“ My dear, as you can-
not afford to buy real
diamonds, you ought to
appearadorned with your
beauty and modesty alone,
which are the rarest orna-
ments of your sex. ”
But she would smile
sweetly, and say :
“ What can Ido ? I
am so fond of jewellery.
It is my only weakness.
We can not change our
Then she would roll around her fingers the pearl
necklaces, hold up the bright gems for her hus-
band’s admiration, saying ;
*‘ Look ! are they not lovely ? One would swear
they were real.”
M. Lautin would then answer, smilingly ;
“You have Bohemian tastes, my dear.”
Often of an evening, when they were enjoying a
tete-a-tete by the fireside, she would place on the
tea-table the leather box containing the “ trash,”
as M. Lautin called it. She would examine the
false gems with a passionate attention, as though
they were in some way connected with a deep
and secret joy ; and she often persisted in passing
a necklace around her husband’s neck, and laugh-
ing heartily, would exclaim: “How droll you
look !” Then she would throw herself in his arms
and kiss him affectionately.
One evening, in winter, she attended the opera,
and on her return was chilled through and
through. The next morning she coughed, and
eight days later she died of inflammation of the
lungs.
M. Lautin’s despair was so great that his hair
became white in one month. He wept unceas-
ingly ; his heart was lacerated with grief, and
his mind haunted by the remembrance, the smile,
the voice—by every charm of his beautiful, dead
wife.
Time, the healer, did not assuage his grief.
Often, during office-hours, while his colleagues
were discussing the topics of the day, his eyes
would suddenly fill with tears, and he would give
vent to his grief in heart-rending smothered sobs.
Everything in his wife’s room remained as before
her decease; and here he was wont to seclude him-
self daily and think of her who had been his
treasure —the joy of his existence.
But life soon became a struggle. His income,
which in the hands of his wife covered all house-
hold expenses, was now no longer sufficient for his
own immediate wants ; and he wondered how she
could have managed to buy such excellent wine and
rare delicacies, which he could no longer procure
with his modest resources.
He incurred some debts and was soon reduced
to absolute poverty. One morning, finding him-
self without a cent, in his pocket, ho resolved to
sell something, and immediately the thought
occurred to him of disposing of his wife’s paste
jewels; for he cherished in his heart a sort of
rancour against the false gems which had always
irritated him in the past.
The very sight of them spoiled somewhat the
remembrance of his lost darling.
To the last days of her life, she had continued
to make purchases; bringing home new gems
almost every month. He decided to sell the
heavy necklace which she seemed to prefer and
which, he thought, ought to be worth about six or
seven francs ; for although paste, it was, neverthe-
less, of very fine workmanship.
He put it in his pocket and started out in search
of a jeweller’s shop. He entered the first one he
saw, feeling a little ashamed to expose his misery,
and also to offer such a worthless article for sale.
“Sir,” said he to the merchant, “ I would like
to know what this is worth.”
The man took the necklace, examined it, called
nis clerk and made some remarks in an undertone;
he then put the ornament back on the counter,
and looked at it from a distance to judge of the
effect.
M. Lautin was annoyed by all those ceremonies,
and was on the point of saying: “Oh ! I know
well enough it is not worth anything,” when the
jeweller said : “Sir, that necklace is worth from
twelve to fifteen thousand francs ; but I could not
buy it unless you tell me whence it comes.”
The widower opened his eyes wide and remained
gaping, not comprehending the merchant’s mean-
ing. Finally he stammered : “ You say . Are
you sure ? ”
The other replied dryly : “You can search else-
where and see if any one will offer you more. I
AT SUNDAY-SCHOOL.
CLERGYMAN": “AND NOW, MY GOOD LITTLE BOY, WHAT ARE YOU MOST THANKFUL
FOR TO DIVINE PROVIDENCE?”
LITTLE BILLY SMITH (promptly): “FOR HAVIN’ BEEN BORN IN NEW ZEALAND IN
THE SAME PLACE AS CARBINE.”
consider it worth fifteen thousand at the most.
Come back here if you can not do better.”
M. Lautin, beside himself with astonishment,
took up the necklace and left the store. He wished
time for reflection.
Once outside, he felt inclined to laugh, and said
to himself : “The fool ! Oh, the fool ! Had I
only taken him at his word ! That jeweller can-
not distinguish real diamonds from paste.”
A few minutes after he entered another store in
the Rue de la Paix. As soon as the proprietor
glanced at the necklace he cried out:
“ Ah, parbleu ! I know it well; it was bought
here.”
M. Lautin was disturbed, and asked :
“ How much is it worth ?”
“Well, I sold it for twenty thousand francs. I
am willing to take it back for eighteen thousand,
when you inform me, according to our legal for-
mality, how it came to be in your possession.”
This time M. Lautin was dumbfounded. He
replied :
“ But —but—examine it well. Until this moment
I was under the impression that it was—paste. ”
The jeweller asked :
“What is your name, sir?”
“ Lautin ; I am in the employ of the Minister of
the Interior. I live at No. 16 Rue des Martyrs.”
The merchant looked through his books, found
the entry, and said: “The necklace was sent to
Mine. Lautin’s address, 16 Rue des Martyrs, July
20, 1876.”
The two men looked into each other’s eyes- the
widower speechless with astonishment, the jeweller
scenting a thief. The latter broke the silence.
“ Will you leave this necklace here for twenty-
four hours?” said he; “I will give you a receipt.”
M. Lautin answered, hastily : “Yes, certainly.”
Then, putting the ticket in his pocket, he left the
store.
He wandered aimlessly through the streets, his
mind in a state of dreadful confusion. He tried
to reason, to understand. His wife could not
afford to purchase such a costly ornament.
Certainly not. But, then, it must have been a
present—a present!—a present from whom . y
was it given her ?
He stopped and remained standing in the
middle of the street. A horrible doubt entered
his mind—she? Then all the other gems must
have been presents, too ! The earth seemed to
tremble beneath him, the tree before him was
falling; throwing up his arms, he fell to tne
ground unconscious. He recovered his senses in
a pharmacy into which some passers-by had borne
him. He requested to be taken to his home.
When he arrived he shut himself up in his room
and wept until nightfall. Finally, overcome with
fatigue, he threw himself on the bed, where he
passed an uneasy, restless night.
The following morning he arose and prepared
to go to the office. It was hard to work after
such shocks. He sent a letter to his chief
requesting to be excused. Then he remembered
that he had to return to the jeweller’s. He did
not like the idea; but he could not leave the
necklace with that man. He dressed and went
out.
It was a lovely day ; a clear blue sky smiled on
the busy city below. Men of leisure were strolling
about with their hands in their pockets.
M. Lautin, observing them, said to himself •
“The rich, indeed, are happy. With money it is
possible to forget even the deepest sorrow. One
can go where one pleases, and in travel find that
distraction which is the surest cure for grief. Oh,
if I were only rich ! ”
He felt that he would like some wine, but his
pocket was empty. He again remembered the
necklace. Eighteen thousand francs ! Eighteen
thousand francs ! What a sum !
He soon arrived in the Rue de la Paix, opposite
the jeweller’s. Eighteen thousand francs ! Twenty
times he resolved to go in, but shame kept him
back awhile. At last he made up his mind, ran
across the street in order not to have time for
reflection, and rushed into the store.
The proprietor immediately came forward and
politely offered him a chair ; the clerks glanced at
him knowingly. .
“I have made inquiries, M. Lautin,” said the
jeweller, “ and if you are still resolved to dispose
of the gems, I am ready to pay you the price I
offered.”
“Certainly, sir,” stammered M. Lautin.
Whereupon the proprietor took from a drawer
18 large bills, counted and handed them to M.
Lautin, who signed a receipt and with trembling
hand put the money into his pocket.
As he was about to leave the store, he turned
toward the merchant, who still wore the same
knowing smile, and, lowering his eyes, said :
“I have—l have other gems, which I have
received from the same source. Will you buy
them also?”
The merchant bowed : “ Certainly, sir.” One
of the clerks left the store to avoid laughing
outright, another pressed his handkerchief to his
mouth. . .
M. Lautin said, gravely : “I will bring them to
you.”
An hour later he returned with the gems.
The large diamond earrings were worth 20,000
francs ; the bracelets, 35,000 ; the rings, 16,000; a
set of emeralds and sapphires, 14,000; a gold
chain with solitaire pendant, 40,000— making the
sum of 143,000 francs.
The jeweller remarked jokingly :
“ The lady invested all her savings in precious
stones. ”
M. Lautin replied, seriously :
“It is only another way of investing one’s
money.” .
That day he lunched at Yoism’s and drank wine
worth twenty francs a bottle. Then he hired a
carriage and made a tour of the Bois. He gazed
at the various turn-outs with a kind of disdain,
and could hardly refrain from calling out to the
occupants:
“I, too, am rich !—I am worth two hundred
thousand francs.”
Suddenly he thought of his official chief. He
drove up to the bureau, entered gaily, saying ;
“Sir, I have come to resign my position; I have
just inherited three hundred thousand francs.”
He shook hands with his former colleagues, and
confided to them some of his projects for the
future; he then went off to dine at the Cafe
Anglais.
He seated himself beside a gentleman of aristo-
cratic bearing, and, during the meal, informed the
latter confidentially that he had just inherited a
fortune of four hundred thousand francs.
For the first time in his life, he was not bored
at the theatre, and spent the remainder of the
night in dissipation.
Six months afterward he married again. His
second wife was a very virtuous woman, but with
a violent temper. She caused him much sorrow.—
From the French of Guy de Maupassant.
Oscar Wilde says that there is only one
thing in the world worse than being talked about,
and that is—not being talked about.
Dear Bulletin, —N.B. to politicians and
others.—Note the result of the recent Congress
elections in the United States. The Democrats
have come off with a whooping triumph, aided by
that recently consolidated but tremendous political
engine, “the Fanners’ Alliance’’—which had ■
massed itself and gave a block vote on the silver
currency extension agitation. That quite de-
tached question is possibly at the bottom of the
whole result, but, of course, it was to be expected
that the Tory and Freetrade press all over the
world would point it out as a living and wonderful
revolt of an entire nation against Protectionist
burdens. There has been a clamorous jubilance
among Australian Freetrade papers, whose crows
still ring in our ears. Suppose we conceded their
contention for purposes of argument, what
would be the first and most vivid illustration to
spring naturally therefrom ? This :If Nemesis
overtook the Republican party on its Protectionist
march, it was because the party “overdid its
part. ” Where public opinion does not back up a
movement to its full length, such movement
should only be hastened so far as, and no farther
than, public opinion backs it up. It is useless
for any legislature to attempt to coerce an un-
willing nation. Apply these reflections to Parkes’
Electoral Bill, now before the House. The
country is “ full ripe ” for one-man-one-vote and
registration reforms. But Parkes offers it all that
and female suffrage and some other things besides,
for which the country is not ripe. I regard
Parkes, in thus acting, as a disguised enemy of
“ one-man-one-vote,” and believe he wants to lose
the Bill, and so to lose it that he can always gas
in its favour afterwards and gain kudos for a
liberalism which he is only fooling. But I would
like to see the Bill read a second time and suitably
amended. This will test Parkes’sincerity.-
-OHIO.

success which has attended the introduction of
Clements Tonic to suffering mankind and the
universal praise bestowed upon it by influential

conclusively proves that this article is undoubtedly
genuine. Clements Tonic has done more good in
the last two years in Australia than all the other
medicines put together can ever claim to have
done, and there is not a city, town, village or
street but what there are people resident in who
are willing to testify to the wonderful efficacy of
this renowned remedy. For instance Mrs. Bowie,

twice.”
The Chief Justice of one of the Australias
has a pet dog that follows him wherever he goes.
As the C. J. is fond of following any member of
the nobility temporarily or permanently located
in his city, there is occasionally quite an interest-
ing procession. The only person really ashamed
of himself seems to be the dog.

Dr. Eben. Atherton, the well-known
Sydney physician, one day not long ago visited a
man who had sustained a tiresome surgical injury,
and who said he was tempted to “finish himseP.”
“Nonsense, my friend!” said the doctor, “you
and I are still good for another thirty years.” A
few days afterwards the patient sent for the
doctor. The servant went and brought back the
answer —“Dr. Atherton is dead.”

tales to Harper and Brothers. He was shown the

One of Australia’s clerical nobility was
Peter Menzies, of Melbourne, and one day he was
gazing on his two beautiful little girls—now, we
hope, two very happy young ladies—when he
exclaimed: “Look here, Brother Iletberingtqn,
it is impossible for me to believe in the doctrine

They are delivered to me pure as snow and chaste

Prescott Belknap, having last month sent
a duel challenge to our old friend Ambrose Bierce,
of the S.F. Examiner, Bierce wrote back ;--“H.
Prescott Belknap, Esq.— Sir : Gentlemen com-
monly obey the laws of their country. It is for
that reason that they find some of them (and the
public sentiment of which they are the outgrowth)
objectionable. If you had committed your felony
in fewer words and better English, your sincerity
would have been as apparent and the temptation
to hand you over to the police more easily resisted.
I must beg you will not strengthen it by further
correspondence.—l am most respectfully yours,
Ambrose Bierce.”
POLITICAL POINTS
Premier Munro maintains that his first
lieutenant (Shiels) must be a Liberal, because he
was born at Collingwood Flat.
Premier Crispi holds that the armaments
of the Great Powers “ will end by ruining Euro p
for the advantage of America.”
Dr. Wallis (N.Z.) has invented a sonorous
phrase, descriptive of the eight-hour workers. He
calls them the “octohorian aristocracy.”
Twenty-nine members oftheS. A. Assembly
vote for female suffrage, 14 aga'nst. This not
being the needful majority, the matter comes up
again.
A Christiana newspaper recently pro-
pounded the wicked conundrum —“Why have
a King?” Things must be moving along in
Norway.
A German Socialist has been awarded
three months’ gaol for prophesying that the
Emperor William would in time become a Social
Democrat.
Melbourne Argus declares that the Ameri-
can elections foreshadow an early “return to
Freetrade.” How can the States “return” to
what they never had ?
Bishop Barry says that “ Socialism, breth
ren, is an error in substituting material for spiritual
good.” The poor are to get in the next world the
reward of their patience.
One rowdy N.S.W. member lately excused
himself by pleading that he couldn’t drink like a
gentleman. And now it is pleaded for another
rowdy : “Oh ! he’s idiotic with drink.”
Premier Munro (who, with his colleagues,
has been returned unopposed) says that in four
years Gillies got £13,000,000 from loans, and left
behind him a debit of £500,000.
Only the other day the Prophet Parkes
predicted that Crick would turn out a model legis-
lator. Henry was slightly out in his tip, however,
for the “ model legislator ” turned out Crick.
Justice Williams (Vic.) says he finds that
whilst the Victorian judges are in favor of estab-
lishing an Australian Court of Appeal, the judges
of the other colonies appear to be opposed to it.
Last week’s cable: “Dillon and O’Brien
have met with a very cool reception in New
York.” This week’s cable ; “ Dillon and O’Brien
at their first Now York meeting, collected £6600. ’>
In Australia children are compelled to
go to school. Why should not adult citizens bo
compelled by law, in the interests of their political
education, to cast their votes at the polling-booth?
“What were the reasons why we turned
out the late Government?” screamed the new
bald-headed Premier of Victoria at Geelong.
“Because you wanted office,” brutally croaked
a dry sepulchral voice.
The S. A. Assembly, we regret to see, has
carried a motion for a railway to W. Australia on
the land-grant principle. And this is the colony
that Henry George alleges has adopted the
principle of the Single-tax !
Parkes’ Electoral Bill does not provide
for the elections being all held on one day ; nor
for annual elections ; nor for the abolition of the
£4O deposit (which even insolvent candidates are
apparently always able to raise).
Crick will again be returned for West
Macquarie. It has been remarked that the
N.S.W. electors never punish any member for
being disorderly—like the Soudan expedition,
disorder is “a good advertisement.”
When the N.S.W. Assembly ruled out
Schey, M.P., for his tedious repetition of the
word “innocuous,” Traill, M.P., suggested that
Premier Parkes should be ruled out for his tedious
and peacocky repetition of the word “I.”
Writing to Figaro, Louise Michel says,
“ Not only do I refrain from asking that women
should enter Parliament, but I demand that the
three or four honest men who remain there should
come out, and that they should no more re-enter
that den of thieves.”
Premier Munro points with pride to what
he evidently regards as a singular circumstance —
the fact that “in no instance in either Chamber
did he apply to any gentleman who refused to join
the Cabinet.” We don’t see any phenomenon in
that. We never offered anyone a fat thing yet
and got a refusal.
Mahuki, the Maori chief, said to his tribe
in the King Country “If you sell your lands
for a price, behold ! you will all become slaves like
the landless white man.” And now the white
land-jobbers, having got a pretext, have arrested
Mahuki. Which is the beginning of a big land-
grab in the King Country.
A London paper says that “ the Per-
manent Under-Secretaryship of the Colonies is a
splendid position. The salary is £2OOO a year,
and the influence enormous.” Which, doubtless,
means that the Permanent one makes a large
annual pile out of the C. M.G. business—for where
else does the enormous influence come in?
Letter addressed by Crick, M.P., to the
N.S.W. Speaker: —
I consider Parliament rotten and corrupt. You put me
nut to-night fearing my tongue and consequent exposure.
1 resign my seat and intend to appeal to my constituents
against a rotten and corrupt Parliament in which freedom
of speech is brutally stifled and suppressed in order to
cover crime.—\V. P. Crick.
A person named Dooley, who sits in the
Tasmanian Parliament, has moved that all
members who have served five years should receive
a free railway pass for life. There is no arrogant
dignity about the politician in these parts. Any-
thing down to second-hand boots or old clothes
comes handy to him, and he doesn’t even mind
having his hair cut at his country’s expense.
The Victorian “Royal Commission on
Charitable Institutions ” has issued a progress
report, which may briefly be described thus
Never give a sixpence to a deadbeat, but refer him
to the Patents and Vaccination Departments;
sell all the hospital and other sites where they
represent big values, and dump down the sick and
the paupers in a fresh lot of humpies somewhere
below low tide.
The N.S.W. Speaker and the Chairman
of Committees derisively deny Mr. Crick’s allega-
tions that they got respectively £2OOO and £lOOO
apiece as bribes in connection with the Broken
Hill Water Supply Bill. It is understood that
the legal firm with which Speaker Abbott is
connected bad a professional retaining-fee, ten-
dered and openly accepted in the usual way, for
seeing the Bill through the House.
Four years ago nine members of the Bris-
bane Assembly voted in favour of the Separation
of N. Queensland, and 40 against it. In the
recent division there were 26 votes recorded in
favour of the Separation, and no more than 32
votes against it. The real numerical strength of
the Separation party in the Assembly at present
is 29, in a Chamber consisting of 72 members,
while in 1886 it was only 9 in a Chamber of 54
members.
The Australian Workman (organ of the
N.S.W. Trades and Labour Council) is about to
be floated into a co-operative daily morning news-
paper. No man is to be employed unless a share-
holder, and no person is to hold more than 20
shares. Shares are to be 10s. each, and 20,000 in
number. The biggest openings in Australian
journalism are for the establishment in Sydney
and Melbourne of uncompromisingly proletarian
daily newspapers.
“ Her Majesty’s Opposition ” is a sample
phrase, which discloses the framework of the
shutter-hole through which the Melbourne Argus
and other Tory papers view all things Australian,
It is with them, always “Her Majesty’s” and
never “the Victorian” Opposition. Munro is
“ Her Majesty’s Government,” and not the Vic-
torian Government, to the Akgus. Parkes is
“Her Majesty’s Government” and not the
N.S. Wales Government with the S.M. Herald.
Mr. Willis, M.P., unknowingly did his
level best in the N.S.W. Assembly the other
night to land his friend Crick, M.P., in a tight
place. There was a motion to expel Crick, and
Willis pleaded that when the latter loudly
charged the Speaker and the Chairman of Commit-
tees with bribery, he (Crick) “was outside the bar
of the House.” Now, Crick at the time was
clearly inside the bar—had he been a single
inch outside, his utterances would have come
within the scope of the libel law.
The great American struggle, Black v.
White, is daily nearing. In the State of Missis-
sippi the negroes’ voting power already exceeds
the white by 50,000 ! “Better a race-conflict and
extermination of one of the races, than to submit
to the rule of the majority of ignorant and bar-
barous blacks,” says a local paper. One remedy
proposed is to allow the white women of the South
to vote to balance the negro vote. Many of the
leading Southern newspapers have endorsed the
idea, but all such schemes are mere palliatives.

Society
They are saying up North that you
should never howl too loudly in public about
how miserable you feel at the loss of your loving
wife’s society, because, after all, the whole truth
concerning the reason of her departure may leak
out. The tongue of society has never before
wagged so furiously as in the matter of this high
and mighty scandal.
# * * . , .
The rumoured reasons why a certain big
official’s wife departed for “Home ” appear to be
getting mixed. One story is that the gentleman
was surprised in the giddy act of hugging a parlor-
maid, and another yarn represents the male party
as discovering something for himself. A third
explanation of the mystery is of a purely medical
character, and a fourth hinges upon a widely-
circulated scandal. The safest thing to suppose
under all these alleged circumstances is that the
aforesaid lady suffers from headaches and wants a
change of air.
# * *
The book that is having the largest sale
among women just at present in Australia is
Tolstoi’s “ Kreutzer Sonata.” Of course each
innocent who buys it imagines it is something
about music—as, of course, it is.
#' * *
It is cabled that Mr. and Mrs. R. L.
Tooth, of Sydney, were guests of the Prince and
Princess of Wales at a ball at Sandringham. This
message simply goes to show that the Tooths of
Tanglefoot Castle have made enough money out
of sheoak to command the worship of the miserable
cableman.
* * *
His Honor, Chief Justice, Acting-Gover-
nor, the Honorable S. J. Way, etc., who is about
sft. 4in. without his wig, does not like to be called
“ The Acting-Governor ”of South Australia. The
order has gone forth that he must be styled “Th®
Deputy Governor.” The. “ Chief Adulator ” once
hinted to one of the officials in the Supreme Court
that in Hingland, dear old Hingland, it was the
custom for the usher to throw open the curtains
shrouding the sacred precincts of the judicial bench
and call “Make way for His Honor the Chief
Justice.” The hint was not taken, and when the
Chief Adulator in that voluminous scarlet robe
(which would fit that son of Anak, Sheriff Boothby)
enters, the tame old gag of “feilentz the usher
always puts in the final “z ”* still startles the
sparrows from their roosts over the canopy in the
Hall of Justice.
* * *
Miss Georgina Watson, of Bendigo, Vic.,
aged 22 or thereabouts, wants to marry Mr. Paul
Kitz, a member of a well-known Victorian family
the head of which was a pioneer of the southern
wine industry. Miss Watson is entitled to £2OOO
a year, but it is provided in her father’s will that
should she marry without the written consent of
her trustees she loses her fortune. _ The trustees
object to give in respect to Mr. Kitz the desired
permission, saying that they have good reasons
(which, however, they refuse to fully disclose) for
their refusal, and now Miss Watson, in asking the
Supreme Court to interfere on her behalf, deposes
that all her brothers and sisters are willing to
accept the man of her choice. An easy way to
settle the matter will be for the Court, which m
such things is omnipotent, to make the trustees
file their ‘objections in order that they may be
judicially investigated. The attention of the
Court could be very properly drawn to the fact
that Watson was a cantankerous old hunks who,
because one of Miss Watson’s sisters married
Mr. “Billy” Murdoch, the cricketer, without
his (Watson’s) consent, left her only a small
annual income, which he stipulated should be in-
creased greatly if Murdoch died, thus making it ol
direct pecuniary advantage to Mrs. Murdoch that
her husband should leave a world which in the
opinion of all good sportsmen and admirers of
pluck is adorned by his presence. The Bulletin
predicts that Miss Watson will in the long run
marry Mr. Kitz, and now proceeds to give the
young couple the very best blessing which can be
obtained in a world which continues to be ruled
from the tombs of cranky old men.
* * *
By the way, the little Bendigo money-
grubber aforesaid, had a somewhat remarkable
signature. It commenced with a thin stroke
running from right to left, through which ran
about a dozen pothooks, and the “signature”
when finished looked like a single panel of a one-
rail fence with split palings on it. J.B.W.’s
“signature” is the only specimen of his cahgraphy
that he left behind—tor no other ever existed, so
far as is known in a very wide circle. Under the
circumstances, one of the trustees’ objections to
young Kitz, that he isn’t of sufficiently good social
position, is simply beautiful,
* * *
This Kitz and Watson case excites interest-
ing and, indeed, valuable physiologico-psychologico
discussions in mixed companies. It will do a lot
of good by rather Balzackian and Zolaistic means.
If a fellow goes acting up at Big Clarke’s in the
Bohemian amateur performances, he will be
dubbed a crank and miss his heiress.
* * *
A well-known Sydney barmaid has con-
fided to an ancient Platonic friend the signs by
which her real “fellow ” may be told. She spoke
thusly—“ There’s the chap who comes in by him-
self and who makes sentimental remarks for 15
minutes over one drink —that’s not the man.
There’s the chap; who comes in with two friends
and makes familiar remarks and prods you with
his forefinger —that’s not the man. Then, there’s
the chap who comes in all alone and sits in a corner
drinking vigorously for two hours, radiant if he
can get a word with you across the counter every
twenty minutes—but that’s not the man. Again,
there’s the chap who walks quickly up to the bar,
calls for a whisky, gulps it down, winks, and
walks out — that’s the man.
* * *
At a religious gathering in Melbourne, last
week, they wanted to dig down and speak a piece,
as Wizard Oil Prince Weston used to say, out of
the Bible, but nobody had one, till a newspaper-
reporter fished a Testament out of his pocket!
Please bracket this with the fact of all the Mel-
bourne reporters being shown over a big brewery,
IN THE GARDENS.
SHE: “I'VE PUT ON A SOFT HAT THIS TIME. ARE YOU SATISFIED!”
and the astonishment of the proprietor when not
one would take a beer.
# * *
A lot of people are laughing over “ The
Career of Lord Borndrunk,” as set forth in an
English paper. All the humour of the thing lies
in the name—the Honorable Ladies Oonsande,
Ermyntrude, and Neredah- —Borndrunk . Lord
“ Borndrunk ” was created by The Bulletin four
or five years ago.
* # *
The Bulletin is glad to recognise in the
claim made by “His "Excellency,'’ Rear-Admira
Lord Charles Scott that he shall have m Australia
precedence over every Australian Governor whom
he meets at dinner, save only the Governor in
whose colony the dinner is held, one of those
ludicrous errors which help on the nationalistic
movement and show how utterly ignorant British
officialdom is of the public feeling of Australasia.
The Bulletin, with scores of thousands of its
readers, doesn’t care a fig for this precedence
question so far as it concerns the right of a foreign
sea-going potentate in gold lace and a cocked hat
who only represents Queen Victoria when he is
shelling coeoanut-plantations in the South Seas,
to “jump” the seat of another potentate who
does represent Queen Victoria for five years m
one of the provinces of the Australian continent.
That aspect of the matter does not trouble us at
all, for in our hearts we care as little for the com-
mon place and prosaic Queen Victoria and her
commonplace relations as they care for us, which
is precious little, save in so far as the selfish in-
terests of their dynasty are concerned. We are
onlv sorry that the dispute has not arisen between,
say, an Australian Premier or Chief Justice and
“ His Excellency ” the British Admiral. We are
now within measurable distance of the time when
our self-elected Premier and President will at
table in our country take precedence over all men.

Sing a song of scotty Scott:
Minor people mayors may flummox,
They will not put on the pot
When it doesn’t suit their stomachs;
He, a man of noble breed,
Proud beyond all common credence,
Sacrifices fizz and feed
On the altar of “ precedence.
What a thing it is to be
Bound by rules and regulations !
Noble tars who plough the sea
Think of rank before their rations;
Never yet for etiquette
Was the hungry bard a stickler —
If his whistle he can wet
Free of charge, he’s not pertiokler.
Blessings on our Gracious Queen,
May her shadow ne’er grow thinner I
In her name a lord has been
Forced to go without a dinner.
Lucky they who know not what
Their position in the State is—
We don’t grumble while we get
Any seat for vittles gratis,

Lady Onslow, the wife of New Zealand s
Gov., gave birth to a son on 14th inst. We hope
that, taking a tip from the Caringtonian Myee,
she’ll call it Hokitika Te Whiti Whakawai
Arawa Onehunga Akarana Ngatimaniapoto
Rangatira Maori Onslow. That would please the
natives and help to strengthen the silken bonds
which, &c.
* * #
The N.S.W. National Club has now prac-
tically “bust,” Sir William Macleay, the
president (who was the club, and “ stood Sam for
it right royally), has, with a number of the more
influential members, sent in his resignation. As
a social institution, the club has now degenerated
into a place where the house-steward daily sup-
plies, at his own risk, a cold eighteenpenny lunch.
MissWllker, the Concord (N.S.W.) mil-
lionairess, has given a cheque for £IOOO towards
the purchase of a steam yacht for mission purposes
in connection with the Church of England Mission
in New Guinea. That doesn’t smack of orange
blossoms, anyhow.
# * *
A Paris jury has acquitted a man who
murdered a widow because she declined to marry
him within twelve months of her first husband s
death. The jury evidently knew a good deal
about widows, and accepted prisoner’s declaration
that the “removed ” lady used to ogle him up to
agony point and then make him raving mad by
prattling about the respect due to the dear
departed. The widow who sits on your lap, and
exhorts you to be patient for the sake of poor,
dear dead Adolphus, is too exasperating altogether.
# * *
Lord Salisbury, it is cabled, remarks that
the proposed visit of the Czarewitch to India is a
sign of peace. Yes, the Russian Bug-Eear will
be minimised by hawking round his cub. It i s
only the unknown that is great or terrible. If
Queen Victoria were to come and walk down
George-st. or Collins-st. with her veil up, the
bubble of distant majesty would burst with a bang;
and if Albert Edward stood for a whole week at
the door of an Australian Mont de Piete, not a
living soul would pass without being firmly con-
vinced that A.E. aforesaid was the public’s uncle.
And if Albert Edward’s eldest son, the alleged
future King of England, stood behind an Aus-
tralian counter, the woman who came to buy
gloves would think that never in all her life had
she been served by so sawney a young giraffe.
* * *
Another building society bung, or bungle.
It is at Bendigo, and the secretary admits plung-
ing for six-thou. It is simply amazing how the
chapel influence in Parliament has kept building
societies from coming under the same inspection
and restrictions as banks.
# « *
Several Melbourne clergymen, who burnt
their fingers badly in the land boom, had the
impudence to lecture the public on Melbourne
Cup gambling. Immortalised in appropriate gold
letters, on a local church foundation-stone, is the
Gold Bug (which his name is Taylor) of that very
same land boom which let so many parsons in.
* * *
It is interesting to learn that Mr. Long,
inspector for the N.S.W. Board of Health, found
56 of the central Illawarra dairies, from which
Sydney derives much of her milk supply, “clean,
but requiring improvement ”—2B “ dirty,” and six
“very dirty.” “Thirty-five of the premises
visited have no closets or conveniences of any
kind, and the numbers in household range from
two to twelve persons.” “An objectionable
practice prevails of leaving milk-cans lying about
the yards and cowsheds ; in many cases Mr. Long
found them lying in manure and dirt.” Ugh ! In
France or Germany quite a number of people
would go to gaol over a matter of this kind.
#* , *
In Lisbon, owing to the antagonism to all
things British, tradesmen have replaced the
announcements “English spoken here” with
“ American spoken here.” Herein is a recognition
of a great and awkward truth. Those who gabble
about the ultimate federation of “the English-
speaking races ” don’t choose to recognise that the
Yankees do not talk English, but talk (and, what
is more, think in) the American language. Nor
will the coming Australian race (the race which
will not be quite certain where its grandfather was
born or what religion he belonged to) talk
English. They will talk, and think in, the
Australian language, the chief characteristics
whereof will be its brevity and its irreverence.
* * *
Ho was a most uncertain person, and as
he was going up for election, two of his friends
we re told off to keep him tolerably sober until
after the poll. Just before mid-day on the date of
]ionimation the guardians aforesaid returned to
head-quarters. “It’s no blanked good,” they
wailed, “no one could keep him sober. He’s
drunk ’a bottle-and-half of whisky this mornin’,
while we’ve only drunk a bottle between us.
# * *
A lecture fully describing Dr. Koch’s new
cure for consumption and his method of treat-
ment is to be delivered at Berlin on 26th inst.
A verbatim report of the lecture should be
cabled to this country. If the press associations
do not undertake to provide such a report, then
the expense should be borne by public subscrip
tion. Perhaps one of the big dailies, the Age,
S.M. Herald, or Argus, will make a plunge on
its own account.
* * *
Water-cycling is now the great British
novelty. How would it suit Sydney Harbour;
You could safely back a blue-pointer shark against
a water-cycle.
* * *
At Avoca (Vic.) a woman of 84 has just
married a man of 54.
* * *
The privates of the N.S.W. Permanent
Artillery were allowed fourpence per day extra
pav while protecting blackleg labour at the mines
a job with which they were heartily disgusted.
On the other hand, those gorgeous creatures, then
officers, were given 10s. daily extra pay while
under canvas, and 16s. daily extra while living at
an hotel. However, we are not sorry to hear this.
It will keep the men’s sympathies on the right
side when the order is given to “Eire low !”
# * *.
The son of a Sydney Judge during the
strike drove one of Lassetter’s drays for pay, out
of sympathy with the employers’ cause. He never
can enter now as an amateur athlete.

# * *
Little sleepy Hobart’s City Council has
decided to light the town by electricity. Marvel-
lous Melbourne, which scorns the island whose
capital is Hobart, and superciliously refused to
entertain the notion of “annexing ” it, still dreams
wer typhoid sowers by gaslight.
» * *
In last Government Gazette (Vic.) is a
detailed statement of the operations of the Albert
Hospital, Melb., which contains a paragraph
giving a list of the patients who died at
jr above the “allotted span” of 70 years,
an d which reads as follows:—“Bricklayer,
a o-e 79, catarrh ; gentleman, 70, inflammation;
lawyer, 80, cancer ; female, 90, senile gangrene ; ”
and so on. We are glad to see a distinction being
made between gentlemen and lawyers, and, in-
ferentially, between “ladies” and “females.”
The hospital authorities are to be congratulated on
the nicety with which they have settled the social
status of their late patients.
# * *
The congregation of East Melbourne
Synagogue recently met together in the Board’s
name to call each other liars, and in a general way
dance upon the injunctions of Moses. Hitherto
the outside world has imagined that when Jews
were concerned in any matter appertaining to
their ancient faith they stood in the corner and
called Heaven to witness that they never carried
on like the Presbyterians, Wesleyans, Baptists,
and similar cornbustionable lambs. Times must
be changing for the worse, however. Up at East
Melbourne the lineal descendants of Abraham
have been shaking their fists, and casting ignoble
insinuations concerning “citrons” at the retiring
chief elder, who wanted to borrow fresh rabbis for
special occasions, and also set up a female choir, a
thing never heard of since the crossing of the Red
Sea. Manwhile the synagogue appears in danger
of going stone-broke without the assistance of the
aforesaid elder, and the whole situation is in-
volved in the bad language and turmoil, usually
associated with primitive Methodism and Chris-
tianity at large. We fear the Jews are becoming
converted.
* * *
The man who marries his first love loses a
lot of fun.
* # *
The S.A. Register is older than South
Australia; the first number was printed in
London. The subsequent numbers were printed
for a long time in a tent on the Adelaide Plains.
Lindsay Cooper, at present on the Register staff,
came out with the first proprietor, Mr. Robert
Thomas, and has been on the paper since the first
copy was struck off.

The committee of a New Zealand chari-
table institution lately instructed its secretary to
sensible, amiable, virtuous, and good-tempered,
advertise for a matron. She was to be accomplished,
The sec. got her, in fact, he married her.
* * *
Melbourne Evening Standard savagely
whoops for a revival of the duello. Perhaps after
the experiences of a dozen challenges daily, which
the staff would most assuredly have to take up,
some modifications might take place in the
organisms so strongly predisposed to atavism.

“An Old Maid with a Beard ” writes to
an English paper to say that she doesn’t use
shaving-soap —simply vaseline and a very keen
razor. Handy woman to have about a house.

map printed on the heading of the European
Mail locates Melbourne on the east side of Aus-
tralia !
* * #
Melbourne sassiety has been enthusing
over a life-size water color portrait of Joseph
Clarke. The Bulletin carries several pictures of
the “Baronet’s ” brother in its mind’s eye. But
not water-color pictures, by any means.
* * *
We got hold just now of an old pamphlet
by an Australian Anglican clergyman in opposi-
tion to marriage with a wife’s sister. It shows
how much professional religion is behind the
times in morality. Our piously salacious author
holds that nothing stops even our most revered
spiritual directors from running amok but a code
of theological laws. “Even a father, embracing
his blooming daughter, is only restrained,” says
the writer, by the red tape of Ecclesiasticism.
Why, the aboriginals really have more morality
than clergymen of this sort.
* * *
The latest reports about the Mormon
abandonment rf polygamy place another com-
plexion on it —that is to say, it is apparently to be
an evasion, instead of an owning-up. Even more
influential than President Woodruff, the Platonic
and “ seventeen - wife - jumping - all-into-bed -at-
once-and-who’ll-put-out-the-light ? ’’-abjuring
cessor of Brigham the Brighamous, is Elder
Cannon, who has four wives. Now, Cannon,
speaking at the great polygamic pow-wow a few
weeks ago, said : “ We give in under compulsion,
with so many millions against us.”
* * *
An Adelaide gentleman is about to be sued
for breach of promise by a lady “ with whom he
first became acquainted when ho rashly saved her
sometimeagofrom a watery grave.’’ This,nodoubt,
is the old, old story with a slight variation. She
was insensible, we presume, when he dragged her
out, and lay damp and motionless in his arms
with her hair hanging down like a sodden horse-
tail, and her dark eyes closed, and a pint of water
in each ear. Also, her hands and feet stuck out
straight in front of her, and there were mud and
prawns in her boots ; and under these painful con-
ditions he didn’t like to leave her to drip on the
sand and walk away. But by-and-bye she revived
sufficiently to throw her wet arms round his neck
and tell him the life he had saved should be his,
and then she put her moist head on his shoulder
and drained in silent resignation down the back
of his neck. Most probably he hadn’t sufficient
strength of mind to tear himself away at this
crisis and let her drop in a shapeless heap of
heartbroken female on the shore, so he stayed on,
and didn’t repudiate the arrangement until it was

* * #
A Melbourne man, charged with nestling
under the bed of a lady hotel-keeper, pleaded that
he had crawled into the wrong room because it
was a peculiarly-built hostel, and his Worship
might have made the same mistake. His Worship
blushed like a poppy and gave the prisoner six
weeks.
* # *
A suburban bar-lounger was the other day
greatly surprised when he saw a prominent blue-
ribbonite who travels on the game hurriedlymopup
an alcoholic beverage at the private window and
depart in peace. Commenting on the astounding
surroundings of this circumstance to thelandlord,
the latter earnestly entreated him not to breathe
a word or he would be ruined. “Lor’ bless you,”
he said, “ I’ve lots of these sorts of customers, and
between ’em they owe me a pretty penny.”
# * *
The latest thing in the way of companies
in Sydney is a proposed Limited Liability concern
to prosecute other L.L. Companies who have
neglected to file an annual summary of capital,
shareholders, &c., in accordance with the Com-
panies Act. The penalty is £5 for every day dur-
ing which default continues, and the promoters of
the new venture, who will play the part of in-
formers, reckon half the fine will come to them.
The claims of the new company for public support
are considerable, and we shouldn’t be surprised to
see a few bishops on the directorate.
*• * *
“Prudery is a perfume which disguises a
vitiated atmosphere.” The Queen of Roumania
is alleged to have written that epigram.
Speaking of the assault on Nationalist
Harrison, M.P., by the Irish police, the Times
calmly says : “ He is a young man, and to have
his head cut open by a bludgeon is only such an
injury as men of his time of life have daily to
encounter in the lawful game of football.”
Colin Mac Master, of Stewart’s Creek, Q.,
who for twenty years has interested himself
greatly in the snake question, concludes that all
snakes have erectile fangs, and that all are more
or less venomous according to the season of the
year. He has no faith even in the carpet species,
popularly believed to be quite innocent, and
advises no one to trust them in the slightest
degree.
It seems very clear now that several
Unionist compositors connected with the N S.W.
Government Printing Office have suffered for
their loyalty to their fellow-unionists during the
strike trouble. A number of others turned
traitors, however, and no sooner did they receive
appointments on the permanent staff than they—
or some of them —sent in their resignations as
members of the Typographical Association.
The latest-discovered “plant” in Pent-
ridge prison is £2OO, in “joey ” notes—“flimsies.”
The warders are all wondering how the bringer-in
evaded the prison-searchers. Bah ! They haven’t
found the lithographing and printing plant yet;
nor the “ accomplices ” outside who “smash ” the
notes, some of which have already got into cir-
culation, Here’s a real live criminal, but what
Melbourne wants is a real live Criminal Investiga-
tion Department.
The Bulletin rises to acknowledge that it
has perpetrated an appalling blunder. A few
weeks ago it stated that Freetrade had so utterly
wiped out British agriculture, and made Britain
so hopelessly dependent on other countries for her
“cheap loaf,” that about six weeks blockade
would starve her into the acceptance of any terms
that might bo offered. Speaking on the same
subject the Age of Steel makes this announce-
ment :
. England is a dependent nation, and with her ports
blockaded and no imports for ten days, the poor of London
would be without a breakfast, and the whole island a poor-
house without a biscuit.
From this it would appear that The Bulletin
was several weeks out in its calculation, and
things are several times worse than it was led to
suppose.*
MEMORIA.
If only in my dreams I may beholfi thee
Still hath the day a goal;
If only in my dreams I may enfold thee,
Still hath the night a soul.
Leaden the hours may press upon my spirit,
Nor one dear pledge redeem—
I will not chide, so they at last inherit
And crown me with the rapture of that dream
Ten thousand blossoms earth’s gay gardens cherish;
One pale, pale rose is mine.
Of frost or blight the rest may quickly perish—
Not so that rose divine.
Deathless it blooms in quiet realms Elysian;
And when toil wins me rest,
Forgetful of all else, in blissful vision
I breathe my rose, and clasp it to my breast!
Florence Earle Coates in October Atlantic.
Celebrities.
It has been a favourite subject with statisticians
to point out the different callings of men calculated
to prejudice or favour longevity. Thus we find it
repeatedly asserted that brain-work and unremit-
ting’ intellectual labour necessarily abbreviate life,
but this is not always borne out by facts. If we
take poets, we find the immortal bard of Avon
called away at 46 in the full development of his
genius. He died in the zenith of his fame, con-
scious of the dawn “presaging a sunburst of pos-
thumous fame.” It is impossible to bring the
language to a more exquisite degree of perfection
than Shakespeare did. In him we find its most
rhythmic genius, the acutest intellect, the pro-
foundest imagination confined in one man, who
truly “ held the mirror up to nature.” In the
famous lines of Dr. Johnson :
Each change of many coloured life he drew;
Exhausted world, and then imagined new,
Existence saw him spurn her bonded reign,
And panting time toiled after him in vain.
Pope, whose unrivalled poetry has charmed suc-
ceeding generations, died aged 56. Byron, who
will live as long as the creations of his genius are
understood, attained the age of 37 ; while Rogers,
the great author of “our eternal destiny,” died at
the ripe age of 93. Turning to German literature
we find that the chill of death palsied the hand of
the immortal Schiller at the same premature age
of 46, at which his great English brother, Shake-
speare, laid down his pen, both dying at the
threshold of their goal, leaving some of their
works half finished. For diction of language and
width of imagination Schiller stands unsurpassed.
Of him it may be truly said
He taught new lands to rise, new seas to roll,
Called into being scenes unknown before,
And passing nature’s bonds, was something more.
Goethe, who as a dramatist, stands still higher
than Schiller, and whose burst of genius is for
ever portrayed in Faust, Egmont, Wilhelm
Meisters, Lehrjahre, and other works, lived to 83.
Klopstock and Wieland, whose creations are full
of magic solemnity, died in the decrepitude of
age. An insight into French literature shows
Voltaire, the nation’s greatest satirist and poet,
wiio in his works discloses the lineaments of his
age, called away at the mature age of 84. Lamar-
tine, the great historian, left this world aged 78.
Calderon, a famous Spanish writer, died aged 87.
Instancing men of science, we find Plato, the
greatest of philosophers, attain the age of 82.
William Harvey, the discoverer of the circulation
of the blood, died aged 79, his famous works but
half completed. _ Among contemporaries we find
the Very Rev. P. Dunne, late Vicar-General of
Albury, N.S.W., who is long past the noon of
manhood, and though ailing for some time is now,
nothwithstanding his advanced age, in excellent
health. This he explains as follows : —I have
used some of Warner’s safe cure while suffering
from pains in the back and loins due to disorder
of the liver and kidneys, and found prompt and
complete relief.” Mr. W. Adams, M.L.A. of
Queensland, who has filled the mayoral chair of
Bundaberg on three successive occasions, though
past the zenith of life, looks well and hearty,
due, as he willingly affirms, to the use
of Warner’s safe cure. The Hon. Geo.
Thorne, ex-premier of Queensland, graduated in
medicine; he retired recently from politics, and
resides in the prosperous town of Ipswich, founded
by his father. Ever at heart the welfare of his
fellow-beings, he says : —“ I have recommended
Warner’s safe cure to many people who have
suffered from different complaints, and in every
case a cure has been effected. Personally I have
used the medicine, and derived the greatest benefit
from it.” Mr. R, 11. J. Reeves, M.H.R., New
Zealand, is of commanding presence, and an able
and popular politician, he endorses the efficacy of
Warner’s safe cure in exceptional terms of praise.
The unanimous verdict of these distinguished
colonists in favour of this specific is convincing
and incontestable proof that it is the surest safe-
guard to vigorous health, as well as the only
means of attaining a joyous and greatly prolonged
existence.

disreputable sons, appears in the number for November 15

My little Maid ’tis you, he said,
Alone are dear to me.
Ah, then, she turned away her head
And ne’er a word said she.
But what he whispered in her ear,
And what he answered too,
' Drink St. Louis Lager Beer,
I love it—so do you.
This little girl you might know,
She was the only daughter ;
I was the only beau
Who bought her Sarsaparilla and Ironwater.

E XPERTS AND CRYSTALS.
Have you ever examined critically the crystals
used in the bezels of the Waterbury Watches? If
not, then this will suggest so doing at your first
opportunity. Each of the glasses used undergoes
a minute and careful scrutiny in the hands of an
expert—an' expert whose precision and untiring
fidelity appear almost marvellous. Crystals are
often rejected whose defects are so slight as to
escape not only the ordinary eye, but even the
vision of foremen and trained employes in other
branches. Yet, upon taking one of them to the
expert, that person will invariably point out the
fault, and do it, withal, so quickly that one would
almost believe the spot to have been marked for
identification. The testing of crystals, rigid as it
is, is but a sample of the method and care pre-
valent in every branch of the Waterbury Watch
Co.’s factory. Precision is the keystone to their
success ; and therein lies the secret of the wonder-
ful results obtained in the time-keeping qualities
of their marvellously reliable watches at a nominal
cost. The people of Great Britain and of the
United States are eminently practical, while those
of the Australasian colonies are nowise behind
them in this particular, and the continued flow of
acknowledgments received from them could not
be wrung forth by misrepresentation, nor sustained
by anything else than the most deservedly practical
worth.
The accurate and reliable Waterbury Watches
may be obtained from any respectable storekeeper
throughout the colony. Price, series “E ” (the
old favourite), 13s. fid'.; series 1 * J ” and 11 L ” (the
new short winding marvels), with enamelled dials.
225. fid. each.
CAUTION. Every watch manufactured by
the Waterbury Watch Co. has the word
*’ WATERBURY ” printed in fine type upon the
dial, and also the interlaced letters “W.W.00,,”
without which none is genuine.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors.
Strictly Original matter contributed to Tiir Bulletin
w {ll be paid for. The Editor will be (jlad to receive and
consider contributions as under :
I. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
g. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
$. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred ).
I. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial ill astral ion.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Sire Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. The
nam?, and address of the writer must appear, in al l
cases, on the, MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecliny rejected Manuscripts,
Ingersoll says, criticising a lady’s article
on modesty in dress: “What a curious opinion
dried apples have of fruit upon the tree l' :

According to the last balance-sheet of
Tooth and Co., Ld,, the big Sydney brewers, they
value their brewery, rolling-stock, and pubs, at
£580,000, and the modest little sum of £300,000 is
owing to them —by publicans, presumably.
The Australian Star proprietors have
started a fond in aid of the distressed strikers. A
very laudable action, indeed, but considering the
tone adopted by the soi-disant democratic paper
during the labour-fight, it looks to The Bulletin
like pouring oil into the wounds of the man you
have just helped to knock down and choke.
Mr. H. H. Champion writes in the Age :
I have a right to point it out to you, because nine weeks
ago I showed you how a frank acknowledgment of your
errors alone could save you, knowing, as I said at the
time, how thankless the task was, and accepting the in-
evitable consequences to myself, for / have seen too much
of workmen not to be aware that they are still in the
stage of intelligence of the baby who, when it burns its
fingers in the fire, vents its spite on the fender designed
to prevent it from the consequence of its want of know-
ledge. lam not prophesying after the event.
There are seventeen more “ I’s ” to follow, but as
that unhappy vowel is feeling very much ex-
hausted, the rest of the essay is held over.
A little suburban paper publishes an
alleged poem by Fitzgibbon, town clerk of Mel-
bourne, on the subject of the strike, What that
cart-horse bard did to the editor to induce him to
print it is not known, but it is presumed that he
either scared him into it or paid for the thing at
advertising rates. Here is a sample hunk
To their saddles the brave farmer yeomen quick spriiw
And their horse-hoofs and sabres right merrily riiw
Out an anthem of freedom from tyrannous cant, °
And wild socialist, nihilist, single-tax rant.
These lines should be tittered in a loud voice to
bring out the full flavour, and the speaker should
assume a martial aspect and pound upon his chest.
ihe other day some of the Walgett
(N.S.W.) Chinese having obtained an order
authorising the exhumation and removal of the
bodies of three of their countrymen, proceeded to
the local cemetery and started'ahead at the resur-
rection business. Shortly afterwards the cus-
tomers of the Chinkies were delighted (or
otherwise) at the sight of the “Johns ” trudging
gaily along the main street, carrying all that was
left of their mouldering friends in the baskets
daily used for hawking vegetables. The first
Ohow who carted his welly ni* cabbage ” round
next morning was fired-out and “ welly ” nigh had
Ins neck broken by an indignant vegetarian, upon
whmh ho hobbled away and informed a friend
that Missee Blank getee too dam ploud since he
come back from Sydney.”
The honesty of the paper run by Nicholas
Willis, A. G. Taylor and Co. is gaugeable from
the fact that it seeks to make The Bulletin
editorially responsible for the opinions expressed
in a correspondent’s letter to this journal. In
double-columning two Bulletin extracts, the
circumstance that one of the cuttings is taken from
the “ Plain English ” column and the other from
“P.J.’s.” letter printed in the same issue is
maliciously suppressed. That is an inferential way
of telling a lie. Then the two extracts are
labelled “ Plain English ” —which is the lie direct
and deliberate. The new organ, being a hopeless
failure in artistic mendacity, had better stick to
the point at issue, which is the transparent selfish-
ness of Taylor, Willis and Co. in bursting-up, for
the sake apparently of £3OO a year and a com-
mercial advertisement, the West Sydney election-
arrangements of the N.S.W. Associated Trades.
It is a noteworthy circumstance that the
man who preaches about the blessedness of poverty
is always an individual with a big salary and a lot
of money in the bank, and the person who wants
to know why the workman isn’t contented never
did any work himself, and. invariably wears a
dress-suit, and goes to the circle at the opera.
“If Providence made me a poor man,” said ?
Sydney employer the other day. “I wouldn’t
waste my time in senseless agitation. I would
rather take a shovel and go digging for any wages
I could get.” And three other employers who
were standing by applauded the sentiment, regard-
less of the fact that the speaker had inherited his
pile from his father, and had done nothing all his
life but accumulate fat, and the last time he saw
his own feet with great difficulty ov'er the horizon
of his stomach was in the blessed year 1879.

To H. P. Kugelmaxn, Esq.
Dear Sir, —This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
tiken ill about three years ago with a sev ere cold, which
t rued into Bronchitis and became chronic. 1 consulted
•more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last i began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter ]
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken had one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time 1 commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coining, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*

BRUCE SMITH.
The Referee
The owner of Cuirassier has been telling some
particular friends to watch that brilliant disappointment
in the autumn, by which time he is expected to be fit
enough to justify his importation and out-Carbine
Carbine.
* * *
An absurd rumor was chucked about after the
Cup to the effect that Donald Wallace intended sending
Carbine “ home.” Of course he didn’t, and if he did, we
wouldn’t let him. The public prefere Carbine to the
totalisator as a money-making machine.
* # *
“ Luck ” is sometimes more apparent than real.
The Bulletin knows a youth who backed the double,
Vengeance and Carbine for the two Cups. After Ven-
geance got in one leg of the double, the youth laid off
£250 out of the £5OO with a second bookmaker. Bookie
No. 1 appears to have departed for regions unknown.
Bookie No. 2 continues to pay and receive—and he wants
that youth’s £250.
* # #
Twenty-four hours after the Cup each of Mel-
bourne’s “popular” barmaids was the proud possessor of
a huge wisp of “Carbine’s tail,” presented to her by one
brainless idiot or another. At the end of the week the
sane man who was asked to admire the fiftieth wisp-
each of the fifty being of different colour and texture
began to wish that “The Cup” was a Cycloramic institu-
tion and that Carbine had never breathed, save only on
the deathless canvas. The mighty intellect that pulled out
some of h s own grey mare’s tail on the road home from
the Cup and gave it to his pet Hebe as a souvenir of Car-
bine’s capillary appendage deserves public recognition in
some way as yet undecided upon. Anyhow, there are
sufficient locks of Carbine’s back-hair on view in Mel-
bourne just now to furnish fundamental fringes for a field
of 39.
* # *
A church-going bookie was absent on a late
Sunday owing to a little affair in which fighting-cocks
and steel-spurs had a big say. The parson missed him—
Stiff-’un always drops a sovereign in the plate —and asked
the missus his whereabouts in the porch. “ John could
not come to church to-day,” she replied. “ The fact is,
he has to do a little betting, but he told me to say he is
with you in spirit!”
* * *
It is appropriately pointed out, in Melbourne,
that Hickenbotham, trainer of Carbine, has been left
almost out in the cold without winnings. They are sub-
scribing for him.
# * *
Joe Thompson once said to a Melbourne sport-
ing man : “ \Ve have always to make out that the
‘ books ’ are hit over the Cup. Suppose I have a matter
of £lO,OOO or £12,000 to get in from small backers—why,
they wouldn’t pay me if they thought I’d made a haul.”
His worst year was Chester, over £12,000 out. After
crying wolf so long, the “bookies” are really hit over
Carbine, and some have to beg time.
* * *
After seeing a large photo, of Carbine, correct-
ing one’s hasty impressions of him in the field, we declare
that none of the engravings are a bit like him, being too
short in the barrel, too small in the head—too fine-drawn
altogether—whereas he is rather a thiokish horse.
* # #
The Duke of Portland doesn’t “tip” his jockeys
anything worth mentioning, on the ground, probably,
that a man who has to be indirectly bribed to run straight
could be bribed to run cronk. It is a sound argument,
and suits Portland’s pocket.
* * *

who drew placed horses in “ Tatt’s ” big affairs this year:

* # *
The famous jumping-horse, Rosebery, surpassed
its former record by clearing 7 feet 1 inch, at Toronto,
recently.
* * #
Harry Dunn, who beat D. C. Ross, wrestling
in five styles, at Dunedin, recently, has been challenged
by a Maori, named Uru. Have you ever read “ Pakeha-
Maori’s ” graphic account of his wrestle with the old-time
chief?
• # #
Goddard has finally refused to sign articles to
fight Jackson in Sydney for £llOO. As he telegraphs
over to say that he is not to blame, an agreement with
some Melbourne club must stand in the way. It is said
that Jackson leaves for America by the next mail. After
the Dooley affair, Peter apparently does not care to fight
the Melbourne referee.
* * *
The N.Z. galloway, Bigness, showed the good
one he is by carrying 6st. lllhs. into such prominence in
the Warwick Farm Handicap. The winner, Dolosa, also
hails from Maoriland, and is a full sister to Whakawai,
one of the sensational horses for the ’B9 Melbourne Cup.
Doiosa’s stable did not plank very much on their chance
as her poor performance at Rosehill on the previous
Saturday ran them off. The drawer of Hugo, the third
horse, in Tattersall’s sweep, offered Giltinan 100 to “ nix ”
and pay all expenses if he would allow Harris to ride, but
Giltinan preferred to take the mount himself. Had Harris
been up, the result might have been different.
# * #
Laing, who, while suffering from exhaustion
after illness, was beaten by Slavin after a tough struggle
in five and a-half rounds, is coming to Sydney from Maori-
land.
* * *

clines to the belief that Ike Weir was handsomely paid to

* # #
Of Police News Fox’s offer to back him
against Slavin for £5OOO, Sullivan says: “Fox’s one
ambition is to find a man to whip me, and in this case he
is trying to bluff me, for he knows that I would not accept
Madden as a trainer. Pox is only after an advertisement.
I shall not fight again, certainly for not less than $25,000
aside. Acting pays much better than fighting. 1 have
made barrels of money this season. You can bet your
life that the stage is a better game than the ring.”

If practice makes perfect, what magnificent
fields the Warwicks should become! On Saturday,
against Belvideres, they put in another afternoon in the
field—Moses and Richardson, 125 and 113, not out. It is
a farce for the Warwicks to play against the young and
vigorous Sy dney' senior clubs. The time has arrived when
they' should dissolve partnership and intermingle with
other elevens.
# * *
Humphrey Oxenham was having a remarkably
busy time on Cup day when a lady speculator kept
bothering him as to what he was laying about certain
horses. Oxenham lost bis patience at last, and upon her
asking him, “ What price Little Bernie?” be replied; “ If
you will only go away I will lay you a hundred pounds to
nothing!”—and the wager was actually booked in her
name.
* * *
For some days previous to the Stanbury-
M’Lcan race it was an open secret that Stan bury was not
himself, and his break-up on Monday showed that there
was truth in the rumour. Stanbury showed better pace
than his opponent, and ere half-a-mile had been covered
it was any odds upon him, but M‘Lean passed him
before the mile beacon was reached, and the race
was then virtually' over, for Stanbury went all to
bits, and McLean had merely to paddle over the rest
of the course. It is said that “Johnny” Deeble wanted
to forfeit, but Peter Kemp thought Stanbury could win
despite his want of condition. Stanbury’s forte is con-
sidered to be strength and staying power, and it is very
unlikely that he was beaten on his merits on Monday last:
* * *
However, there is this to be remembered :
Hanlan emphatically prophesied that M‘Lean and no one
else was the coming champion.

Baron started at 3 to 1 for the Hurdle Race at
Warwick Farm on Saturday, and was winning easily, when
he rushed the first hurdle in the straight and came a
cropper, young Kelso narrowly escaping severe injury.
He was sufficiently recovered, however, to ride in the last
race.

the latter while swinging a blacksmith s hammer. By
the way, the Australian Athletic Club Hall (late Darling-
hurst Skating-rink), where the contest takes place, is said
to be the largest and best fitted-up institution of the kind
in the world. It contains at one end a billiard-room with
two tables, a bar, and reading-room with piano ; at the
other end, bath and dressing rooms. The building wid
seat several thousand people, and is fitted-up with all the
latest gymnastic paraphernalia. In the basement is a
skittle-alley and rifle-range. Larry Foley, Maber, a
gymnastic teacher and a very obliging secretary are in
constant attendance.

In the far, far distant past, when old “ Merks ”
such as “ Tom ” Strange, “ Ted ” Hall, “ Jim ” Mackenzie,
“Nick” Lockyer, “Joe” Smart, “Alec.” Finlayson,
“ Jack ” Wilkinson, “ Joe” Walford, “ Bod ” Keddie, and
other old hands were young at the game, the writer recol-
lects “Davey ” Fletcher as an active member of the Mer-
cantile Rowing Club. “ Davey’s ” record is phenomenal.
He has started in three hundred and fifty-two, or there-
abouts, races, and he is still a “ maiden ” and an active
member of the Mercantile Rowing Club. On the afternoon
of the 6th instant Mr. Fletcher rowed a private match
against Mr. M‘Keown, another member of the M.R.C., and
the writer is pleased to say that Mr. Fletcher was beaten,
for it would indeed be a pity, after seeing out two genera-
tions of rowing-men, for him now to break his record. It
is estimated he has covered over a thousand miles in his
various races, and never once during his rowing career
has he allowed an opponent, no matter how large the
field, to finish behind him.

Blackham attributes the gruelling the Austra-
lian cricketers received to the g'reat improvement made
by their opponents since the last team’s visit. Just so.
And M'Auliffe lost his fight because Slavin punched too
hard, and the “Cup” didn’t go to Warwick Farm because
the other horse ran too fast, and divers other things have
happened simply because the best have proved to be
better than the worst.

Joe Thompson was the most extravagant of all
the bookmakers in laying the Cesarewitch and Cambridge-
shire. His price was 250 to 1. You could have got 1000
to 1 that double 20 years ago.
# * %
Many of the spectators of the Griffo-Moran
fight for the feather-weight championship voted Moran a
muff, but, as a matter of fact, he is a remarkably clever
boxer, while Griffo is a phenomenon. When the pair
first nut up their hands, Moran started to make play, and
on a less clever boxer he would very likely have got home,
but Griffo was never where Moran thought he should be,
and when the little wonder commenced to play a tattoo
upon poor Moran—popping in one-two-three without a
return —he reminded the writer much of George Seale.
Like the latter, Griffo is not a smasher of the Fogarty
order, but is a dreadful punisher, worrying his mandown,
and it was not till the thirteenth round that Moran
received his quietus. Griffo is a remarkably big little
man with a chest which looks abnormal in its girth, and
the writer has an idea that when lie has done with the
feather-weights, he will give the light-weighty all their
weight in and a big contract, should he have a shy at
them.
# * *
Young Griffo was at Warwick Farm on
Saturday, and did not carry a mark from his meeting with
Moran the previous night.

A gruesome memory of old Sydney is
about to be obliterated. The old house at the
corner of Margaret and Kent streets, in which
Knatchbull murdered Mrs. Jamieson in 1844, is
going to be pulled down.

H.M.S. Serpent, new steel cruiser, like
our new Anglo-Austral ones, has, it is cabled,
been lost. Fortunately for himself, the designer,
Sir Cooper Key, has handed in his checks to the
Sky Admiralty lately. Coles, designer of H.M.S.
Captain, was lost in her. The most awful ex-
perience in the life of any man was when Sir Thos.
Bouch, designer of the old Tay Bridge, heard it
had tumbled with a crash, taking with it a train-
ful of people. Just imagine the messenger coming
in, with a face like a blank sheet of paper, while
Sir Tom was telling a good story over the wine.
“I’ll be with you again directly, gentlemen,”
says he, puffing his cigar and laughing. But he
never laughed more. He carried a big stone on
his soul for a month or two, and died—pitied.
