A Poet’s Dinner.
“Love is the Poetry of the Senses.”— Balzac.
In those days I was very poor and very amorous'
My black coat was so threadbare, the cloth was
so worn, that I dared not put it on or take it off
without the greatest precaution. The knees of
my pantaloons shone like satin, my hat was at the
age when little girls know how to fib, and, as to
my boots, modesty prevents my telling by what
ingenious efforts I managed to keep the incon-
stant soles.
All my fortune was composed of some manu-
scripts in prose and verse, which had already
made me the terror of a number of editors.
Oh, I was truly very poor and very amorous !
When I have said that I wore my hair long,
falling on my coat-collar ; that I was slender and
delicate, that I had a pale face, a large nose, and
ecstatic eyes, one can see what a sorry figure I
made.
It was the first night of a new play at the
Comedie Erangaise. I knew the Princess Lou-
banoff would be there, and I would sooner lose
one of my eyes than this opportunity of seeing—
though it might be but for an instant —the radiant
creature who had captivated my soul. Luckily I
possessed the sum necessary to pay for the modest
place I longed for, and a small surplus. Dinner-
time approached. I was hungry, and, in haste to
get rid of that prosaic need, I spent my surplus
money for some fried potatoes, intending to drink
at the first street fountain I came to. What could I
desire more ?
But I had reckoned without the most cruel of
my faults —that which has always poisoned my
existence —pride. I could never say which was
the more painful to roe, to be poor or to appear so.
I had no sooner got my paper horn-of-plenty
full of potatoes in my hand than I became much
embarrassed, imagining that everybody looked at
me with compassion. However, I lived too far
away to think of returning home.
“Bah!” I said to myself; “they may think
what they like. lam going to eat my potatoes in
the face of the passers-by !”
And as I walked I began my repast. But I had
not swallowed the first mouthful when a beautiful
young girl, coquettishly dressed, passed close to
me and looked at me. There was so much kind-
ness in her gaze, her pretty eyes expressed so well
her regret at seeing me dine so poorly, that
nothing more was needed to completely overcome
me. Ashamed, I lowered my l head, and, feeling
myself redden to the ears, I passed very quickly.
I had scarcely taken three steps when, yielding to
I know not what sympathetic influence, I turned.
The young girl had turned at the same instant,
and for one second I met her sweet glance. Evi-
dently I had inspired her with interest, perhaps
with pity. Much mortified, I hastened on, avoid-
ing crowded streets.
If I had listened only to my vexation I should
have flung my potatoes in a corner, but my
appetite having mastered my self-love I sought
some quarter where I might shelter myself from
the stares of the curious, and chose a gateway
which was completely deserted. I sank down
there, and leaning against the wall near a flight
of steps I again attacked my fried potatoes, which
seemed to me delicious. There, at least, I might
hope calmly to finish my repast, and, again seized
by the sweet, by the intoxicating obsession which
pursued me everywhere, I plunged into contem-
plation of my love. “In a few hours,” I said to
myself, with joyous little thrills, “in a few hours
I shall see her ! Perhaps as she goes through the
lobby she may give me a lovely smile,”' And
then, in imagination, I saw her fine lips move,
while her large eyes half veiled themselves. Oh !
I knew exactly how she smiled, how she walked,
with what dainty airs she turned her head to
listen. I could imitate her slightest gestures,
repeat her flute-like intonations, modulated like
the warbling of a nightingale; I even owed to her
the habit I had of passing the tip of my tongue
over my upper lip. But what I could never find
words to express was her turbulence, as of a spoiled
child, the cajoling, almost familiar ways with
which she glided into the heart of everyone and
coolly installed herself there forever. She was a
true, an exquisite Parisian, the widow of Prince
Loubanotf —a widow of 23 years, with the intact
freshness of a young girl, and with eyes possessed
as by an evil spirit with insatiable coquetry.
I would have given my life to kiss her hands.
At home I had arrived at kneeling down to think
of her, Certainly I vras insane.
It was hinted that the princess was not a dragon
of virtue, and ill-disposed tongues pretended that
she was singularly capricious and perverse, But
what could I hope from so grand a lady—l, most
pitiable poet, when her beauty, her riches, her in-
dependence, drew around her a throng of the most
powerful, the most illustrious adorers ? It is true
that since I had been presented to her, that is for
six months, she had shown interest in me and
never seemed annoyed to see me pale and tremble
everytime she spoke to me. She would allow,
discreetly allow me to die of love for her, as she
permitted the flowers that adorned her breast to
die in exhaling their soul of perfume. That was
all.
I had not yet eaten a quarter of my fried
potatoes when I was obliged to pause in my
repast. There was the rustle of a dress on the
steps. I hid my cornucopia, and assuming the
indifferent air of one who waits, I did not move.
But the nearer that rustling of silken draperies
approached my heart beat more violently without
any reason. A sort of instinct told me something
my spirit could not know. Suddenly a radiant
vision agitated my whole being; a form, a
brilliance, a perfume familiar —beloved ! I closed
my eyes.
Then a golden voice said in my ear, “Ah, my
dear poet, how delighted I am to meet you !”
I had barely time to slip my horn of fried
potatoes into my coat pocket.
“ Madam—l bless the chance I”
“ I was thinking about you.”
“Really !”
“ I was going to write to you.”
Unable to master my emotion I could only
stammer incoherent phrases.
The princess, on the contrary, talked volubly
and appeared enchanted with the meeting.
“ They have sent me a Muscovite romance. I
am enthusiastic over the music. You know
Russian. I have counted on you to translate the
words.”
“ I am at your service.”
“ Come quick ; I will take you.”
I opened my eyes very wide. “You will take
me ? ”
The princess had drawn me to her carriage. She
sprang lightly in, and making a place for me,
“ Come, get in,” said she.
I was astounded. And thinking of what was
in my coat pocket, “Oh, no, madam, I can-
not ”
“Why not ?”
“ I did not expect ”
“ How boyish you are! ” she said, smiling.
“ Get in ; I command you,”
It was necessary to obey, An instant after I
felt myself borne rapidly along. I was dizzy. My
emotion was so great that I could not breathe.
The princess doubtless perceived this, for again I
saw her smile. Wishing to increase my confusion
she leaned toward me.
“You were waiting for me just now?” she
asked sweetly.
“ Oh, no, madam, I swear to you ”
“ Then what were you doing on the steps of the
Baron de Lucques ? ”
What answer could be given ? Could I own that
I had taken refuge there to eat my fried potatoes?
I blushed furiously without being able to think of
the smallest fib, and the princess could only con-
clude that I had followed and waited for her. It
did not appear to displease her, for she presently
began again :
“Do you know, sir, tha I have great compli-
ments to pay you ? I have read all your poems,
all, and I have found them very beautiful.”
“ Ah, madam, is it true ? ”
“ Really they have touched me. You will pub-
lish them ? ”
“ Never,”
“ What, never ? You will conoeal them from
the public ?”
“ It is enough for me that you have read them.”
“ That is almost a declaration, sir,” she cried ;
“ your poems are all of love ! ”
I remained voiceless, but my eyes were so
eloquent that the princess had to lower hers.
“ Ah, you poets,” she resumed, “ what beautiful
loves you have in your heads ! ”
“In our heads,” I cried. “Ah, madam, be
persuaded that one never talks well of love except
from his heart, and if I were a woman it seems to
me that I could not be deceived.”
The subject of conversation was not of a nature
to make me find the time long. We reached the
avenue d’Eylau as by enchantment. The carriage
entered the courtyard and I helped the princess
to leave it. I kept her little hand in mine as we
went up the stairs, a vast staircase, mysterious
like a temple, and covered with carpet where our
footfalls made no sound. We had talked of noth-
ing but love, and I was so full of my subject, I
expressed myself with so much warmth, that a
sweet intimacy was not slow to establish itself
between us. We had ascended a dozen steps and
found ourselves under the light of the candelabra
when the young lady stopped.
“Then,” she said in a low voice, “you, too,
you love me ?”
“ That love is all my life, madam !”
“It is easy to say that one loves—but, the
proof ?”
“ The proof ?”
I still held the hand of the princess, who looked
into my eyes as if to read my soul.
“ Yea,”' she repeated, “ the proof.”
Had I the time to improvise an ardent plea, to
tell her all I felt, to multiply words ? It was im-
possible. I was wildly excited; a brilliant idea
crossed my mind. And then her eyes were so
alluring ! Yielding to the irresistible temptation
I caught her in my arms, and, for an instant, my
lips sought hers.
At last they met ! Oh, that was a kiss of
despair ; a supreme kiss wherein I seemed to lose
my life ! It was no longer the princess palpitating
in my strong arms, but the beloved woman who
thrilled at my touch and whose soul I drank. I
do not know how long it lasted; we whirled upon
the steps.
Then I saw the princess clinging to the gilded
railing, very pale, her eyes closed. She had one
hand on her breast and seemed no longer to
breathe. As for me, I trembled like a criminal.
When she opened her eyes she frowned at first,
Then her face softened and I saw that she tried to
smile.

“'You use singular arguments,” she said in a
light tone, from which it was impossible to divine
what was passing in her mind.
At this moment we heard the sound of a door
that some one opened! The waiting.maid of the
princess came to tell her mistress that many callers
awaited her. , „ t ,
They were Madam Gravere de Miret and her
three big bachelor sons, the handsome Duke de
Maules, the little Vicomte de Vombelles and the
lively Marquise de Musirolles. The latter ran to
embrace the princess.
“Ah, my dear,”said she, “you did not expect
to find such a large court. Nobody wanted to go
without having seen you.”
The princess would not allow them to depart
immediately, and, with her bewitching grace
began to chat, saying all the time the prettiest
things. _ _ .
I was very little acquainted with this brilliant
company, and the lovers of the princess especially
cast upon me stealthy looks, full of defiance and
jealousy. But I gave no attention to them nor to
the conversation, interesting as it was, because a
small personage to whom I had not yet been pre-
sented was all the time causing me the most cruel
alarm. This was Monsieur Bonbon, the dog of
the princess, a griffin of pedigree, a rare and mar-
vellous beast as large as my two fists and who had
Cost tile price of an ox. It inconceivable to
What extent these little ahimals, reared in
Opulence, call become foppish and ridiculously
Vain. There is something of the vulgar upstart
in their small heads. When I first entered he
sprang from the armchair where he was coddling
himself in soft drowsiness and came straight to
me, eyeing me from head to foot, showing by most
expressive grimaces the disdain and astonishment
inspired by a visitor so ill-dressed. “ Why
do they admit people of that sort?” he
seemed to say. Then, running around me, he gave
a long growl, which doubtless contained all the
abusive language that a dog could utter to a man.
My boots, above all, appeared to him monstrous.
He touched them with the tip of his paw, smelled
at them with a disgusted air, and carried his in-
solence so far as to protend to sneeze. At last he
turned his back on me and walked off, stopping
now and then to look sideways at me. It was
plain that he would have thrown me out of the door
if he had been able. Hoping that Monsieur Bon-
bon would cease his impertinences I took a seat.
He was placed on the knees of his mistress, and
indifferent to the caresses of the pretty hand that
patted him, he gave me ugly glances that did not
alarm me. Alas, he had other malice in store for
me. Presently he raised his head, his nostrils
dilated right and left, he gave a series of little
sniffs as if he inhaled an unaccustomed odor.
“ What is the matter with him now ?I asked
myself uneasily. Then a subtle emanation sur-
rounded me. Gradually the scent became stronger
and more noticeable. Suddenly I comprehended
the frightful truth. The fried potatoes I had in
the pocket of my coat were tainting the drawing-
room. No words could express the anguish into
which this discovery plunged me. I might hope
that the delicate people around me would not
suspect the truth, but Monsieur Bonbon was not
to be deceived(| Whether he was allured by the
odor and merely yielded to his greediness, or in
his ill-nature took a spiteful pleasure in denounc-
ing me, the detestable dog leaped to the floor and
bounded toward me. Then, standing on his hind
legs, he sniffed at me from every side until he had
discovered the pocket of provisions, upon which
he made a desperate attack.
I tried to seize him ; he escaped me. I tried to
e£, E,rpLr s
determined to see what I had in my pocket, and
nothing could make him abandon that idea. It
was necessary to take a heroic stand. I rose, and,
bringing under me the dangerous skirt containing
the rest of my dinner, I sat down on it to both
shelter it from the enteipnsing Monsieur Bonbon
and stifle the betraying perfume,
Then my ferocious persecutor jumped on a
neighbouring chair and yelped witn fury. After
that he began to scratch ray thigh with his fore-
pawj-* X id
mentally, looking at the accursed brute with a
sinister eye. General attention was attracted to
me. aiUiSe BoSon ?” the princess had
already been asking. She came to my side to
take rum. . . „ T
Now she will smell the fried potatoes, i
thought, becoming scarlet. ■ ;ii
* or a moment 1 had an idea of making a wilu

She herself, placed the music on the rack and
I sat down at the piano. It was a melancholy
popular song that I had le %rn^ddurn g t

Wl fh. ln Y sensibility. .
1 here was a general fluttering murmur; my
slight voice had moved all hearts. I was

Monsieur Bonbon was renewing his hostilities, I

T he infernal animal had had tune Ibo ruram gein
my pocket and liad dragged put the hoin of
plenty . Now he held it in his tiny jaws an 1
shaking it violently, scattered the fried-potatoes
on all sides on the carpet. .
Overwhelmed with confusion, I remained dumb-
founded a ved before my eyes, a ringing in my
ears. I had a vague sense that there were exclama-
tions of surprise and ill-suppressed laughs i hen
there was a general retreat. Alone with the
princess, I rose and made some unsteady steps
towards the door. She stopped me.
“You will dine with me i” she asked in a voice
sweetly earnest.
This last blow finished me. Could she not
understand that at such a moment an invitation
to dine resembled a charity ? I wanted to speak,
but I was suffocating,
As she drew nearer I turned my head away to
conceal from her the tears in my eyes, and I rushed
away, overcome with shame and grief,
I lived in a pretty little room in the top storey
of a high house. That is where I went to abandon
myself to despair. I flung myself on my bed,
crushed under the breaking up of my dreams,
The dreadful idea that I had been ridiculous
before the woman I loved was-continually crossing
my mind. Now the princess knew both my
misery and my love. That kiss I had stolen from
her upon the stairs she might otherwise have been
able to pardon, but how allow such audacity on
the part of a man who carried his dinner in his
coat-pocket ? For half an hour I sought vainly
for a little relief to my chagrin, and with sorrow-
ful soul I was looking upon the future as a black
immensity without stars, when there came a gentle
knocking at my door.
I started. Then sending the visitor to the
infernal regions I went to open the door, resolved
to quickly get rid of the importunate person.
But as soon as I saw the new-comer I drew back
in amazement.
“ You !” I cried.
“ Yes.”
“You, princess; you here?”
She appeared much moved, and her voice
trembled.
“Yes,” she said with a divine smile._ “You
would not dine with me, I have come to dine with
you.”
“To dine !! !”
With this exclamation I looked about me
vaguely imagining that since the princess was
in my quarters it would be no more marvellous to
see my attic transform itself into a banquet-hall.
“ Do not be uneasy,” she continued, “foreseeing
that you would not be prepared for my visit I
have brought all that is necessary.”
It was not until then that I noticed she had
something in her hands, and, again wounded in
my pride, I frowned in abominable fashion.
“ Oh ! I beg of you,” said she, very humbly and
very sweetly, “don’t be vexed, don’t send me
away; let me satisfy a caprice.” Then going to
the table she unfolded a large newspaper. “ See !”
said she.
I uttered a cry of surprise. She had brought
nothing but a horn-of-plenty filled with fried
potatoes.
“ I know very well,” "said the gracious woman,
“it is the dinner of a poet and that I am un-
worthy ; but for this time the Muses may allow me
to take part.”
I threw myself upon my knees and covered her
hands with kisses. “Oh! how I love you!” I
sighed.
“ Nonsense ! Get up and lay the table-cloth, I
am dying of hunger.”
I could not believe in so much happiness. “ You,
you, princess—it is really you ? ”
She took off her hat and cloak. “It is very
nice here; you must have a superb view. I
should like it here myself, if they would let me
live so.”
“ You want to make me crazy ?”
She went and came lightly as a bird, animated
by infantine curiosity.
“ You know,” said she suddenly, “ the Marquis
de Musirolles has often begged me to give him
Bonbon—well, to-night I sent the dog to him. ”
And without giving me time to speak ; “ Will you
allow me to open that closet? Napkins, good ;
that is all I wanted—don’t disturb yourself.”
With adorable gravity she spread a napkin on the
table as a cloth and opened the cornucopia which
contained the fried potatoes.
“ Please, princess, let that be.”
“ Not at all, I am going to keep house.”
“You will soil your dress.” Possessed by I
know not what demon of coquetry, she cast aside
a lace shawl and revealed her beautiful bare
shoulders and arms. What a radiant apparition !
Transfigured by the light of her dazzling charms
the exquisite creature seemed tome to be endowed
with supernatural loveliness. I clasped my hands
in transport of admiration, and, with beating
heart and eyes rolling in ecstacy, I was about to
pour forth my love when she put her little hand
over my mouth. “I know it, I know it,” said
she, “it’s why I came. Tell me, where are the
glasses ?”
“ The glasses ? I have but one !”
“ But one !-oh ! oh !” Then, with a gesture of
contempt, “Bah!” 1
Having found out that the pitcher was full 0 t
pure water, she placed it beside the glass and
assuming a satisfied air : “There!” she cried “T
have forgotten nothing. Come, then, all
ready.” 18
She flung herself upon the sofa and at once
with the mien of a gourmand, daintily dipped her
slender, pink fingers into the fried potatoes and
began to eat, crunching them as if she had never
tasted anything more delicious. I sa t down
beside her and imitated her.
“ Do you know that I bought them myself?”
“ You, princess !”
She tried to imitate the manner and speech of
the shopkeeper, but ended in a wild burst of
laughter. Then, having filled the large gl ass
full of water, she drank half and offered me the
rest.
“Drink,” said she, “drink.” I emptied the
glass with one draught and resumed my absorbed
contemplation of the beautiful creature.
“What is the matter with you?” she asked.
“I am intoxicated.”
“Already! Gracious! What will it be when
we have finished the pitcher ? Come, eat.”
“lamenting,” I said, devouring her with my
eyes. “ Come, be wise, it is necessary to dine.” 3
But it took us not less than four hours to finish
our fried potatoes.

Says the Dean of Rochester of the Prince
of Wales, “ I do not read in history of any prince
who has shown a more earnest, practical, self-
denying interest in the welfare of the people than
His Royal Highness.” That’s not saying much
for Tummy, but the Dean then proceeds to call in
this “potential purifier” to_“say the word and
lead a crusade against gambling and betting” that
will straightway “purify premises in comparison
to which the Augean stables of Hercules were
bright and clean as the deck of a warship and
fragrant as the summer rose.” Hum ! The Dean
selects the Prince as a social purifier on the same
principle that the dirtiest man in town usually

Tales of “The System.”
No. XIX.—The Procession of the Buttercup.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Not very many weeks ago, just as “ yellow-
haired September ” began to trail her perfumed
garments over the wide Australias, but while
wintry snows were still lingering on the leonine
outlines of Mount Wellington, there “ fell asleep”
a dear old lady, out of whose heart had never died
the scent and colour of Life’s spring-time, though
her noble head had been bleached by 90 years,
whose calendar, alas ! had counted out to her
many more winters than summers.
In the same room that she had slept in (except
during an occasional brief absence) for over half-a-
eentury ; with the window open so that she might
see with her dying eyes the slumbrous Lion on
which for all these years she had loved to cast her
waking glance; with the curded foam of the
Derwent bursting into sibillant echoes on the
Bellerive beach, and breathing into her ears the
sighs of long ago; with her children and grand-
children and great-grandchildren about her, pray-
ing blessings on her for giving them a priceless
example of saintliness in a wicked world, she
passed away.
And thus I am now at liberty to repeat the story
that, sitting one evening with her on the verandah
of her villa, some ten years since, she told to me.
I had been inspecting, during the afternoon,
the little structure at which that most genial of
companions, and most indifferent of clergymen,
the Rev, Robert Knopwood, had, at one - time,
enjoyed the incumbency, and I mentioned the
circumstance to ray gracious hostess.
“I knew Mr. Knopwood very well,” she said.
“When I lived at Glenorchy in 183— he fre-
quently dined with me. He was a curious kind
of clergyman. To the very last —he died in ’3B
or ’39, I think—he preferred a good glass of wine
or a run after kangaroo to officiating in church.
But his faults were those of his day ; and his
virtues were not those of his time, for in dealing
with prisoners, he had all the kindliness of the
present day and not the disposition of his own
period.”
“I am glad to hear that,” I remarked. I had
formed a different opinion of him from his diary,
and what some old residents have told me. Come,
cannot you tell me an anecdote or two about
him?” -
She thought for a few moments, and then said,
“ There is one little incident which I will relate,
as it will show you his thoughtfulness for the
convict-class. But—but you must promise me
not to print it!”
“Why not, my dear madam?” I answered.
“You know I am gathering material for literary
use by-and-bye. And if the incident is evidence
of Mr. Knopwood’s humanity, by all means, it
should be told, if for no other reason than that it
will supply a contrast to the characteristics of the
majority of the officials of the Penal System.”
“ Indeed,” she said, sighingly, “that is only
too true !” And then, with a delightful smile
playing all too evauescently on her sweet face,
she continued, “Well, if you will print it, you
must not do so till after I am gone, for, you must
know, I figure in the story.” . .
And then she told this tale. I print it sub-
stantially as I took it down from her own lips.
Such additions as I have made to it are gathered
from equally-authentic sources, and are in ampli-
fication of her allusions to her own and her hus-
band’s character—points on which, in her humility
she was naturally reticent. .
As I went from her home that night she took a
bloom of fragrant myrtle and gave it to me. I
pinned it to the manuscript of her story. Now, a
decade later, I unpin the withered blossom, and I
find it has left a mark on the paper that emits a
faint perfume. So her life left its impress upon
the early pages of a colony’s history, and the
fragrance of her presence and her actions will endure
long after those who knew her personally have
followed her to the grave.
PRICE WARUNG.
I told you the other day (she began) about
Janet Guilfoyle’s thrush.* That was a true tale
of a bird’s influence over hardened and reckless
natures. -My story now is of a simple flower, the
simplest of all flowers—nothing more than a
common buttercup, and the magic it worked.
L ,my husband, and I, were living in a
small cottage at Glenorchy under the shadow of
the great mountain, in 182—. There it was the
Rev. Mr. Knopwood used frequently to visit us. ,
I fancy I see the old man riding up to the palings,
dismounting from his old white hackney, with a
rusty surtout dropping to his ankles, and with as
rusty a tall hat covering his grey hair, and coming
up the pathway past our tiny lawn with an
ambling trot not unlike that of his old pony.
Well, on one of these visits, as I was waiting to
receive him on the raised porch, he stopped in the
middle of the pathway and bent his gaze on the
lawn. Then I saw him, quite regardless of my
neat box-border, step from the pathway to the
lawn centre and kneel dowm.
The thought flashed into my mind that the dear
old gentleman had gone mad, and I called for
L . He came, and was as amazed as Ito see
the venerable clergyman kneeling upon the sward
and thrusting his face to the grass. L went
down the steps, and saluted Mr. Knopwood. The
old minister —we used to call him the “Ancient ”
—looked up and said : “ Do you know what you’ve
got here ? Real English buttercups and daisies !”
He spoke quaveringly, just as though he were on
the verge of crying—as, indeed, he was ! “No !
said my husband, and he threw himself as eagerly
on the grass as the “Ancient” had done. I ran
down the steps, and bent over the two men. Oh,
the thrill that went through me as 1 saw the
simple, homely buds, the pinkish-white discs and
the yellow cups, just glimmering amid their sheath
of green grass ! and I, too, must kneel down and
bury my face in the delicious dampness —just like
the two other fools! For fools we were—l was
actually crying, and the men pretending to laugh
at me, but* all the time with great tear-drops in
their own eyes. And all because the field-flowers
spoke to us of home—of the old land that none of
us were likely to see again, of its meadows and
lanes, of its gardens and hedges, of its old castles
and itp mountains, and rivers and lakes !
The wonder was how the flowers got there.
Wild flowers, native to the island, there were in
abundance, and scores of varieties of the sweet
old garden-flowers had been brought out by
many, but it was so improbable, we thought, that
anyone would ever take the trouble to bring over
twenty thousand miles of sea so common a thing as
a buttercup root. At last, some days afterwards,
we learnt the truth. The Irish doctor of a
convict transport had brought out with him
a square yard of Irish turf. He had heard of that
story of Sir Henry Hayes importing some Irish
soil to Sydney, and it put into his head the notion
of taking out with him some of the “ould soil.”
And so lie had a box specially made, and from a
field near his native village he dug up a solid block
of earth, and carried it away with hiin. On
arrival at Hobart Town he determined to settle in
the colony, and he had become the tenant of our
cottage —we followed him in its occupancy—and
in the centre of its diminutive sward had re-
planted the piece of turf.
Mr. Knopwood had discovered these particulars
through talking about the flowers in the city. One
who knew the doctor told him, and he repeated
the account to us. And from the <fid minister’s
talking, curious things— you may term them silly
—resulted. We were fairly pestered out of our
lives by visitors of all classes, both “free” and
“ bond,” who came to see the flowers while they
lasted. Big, ruffianly fellows, with the regular
convict face, holders of ticket-of-leave, would
come and stand for hours together and look at the
bobbing buttercups over the palings—the butter-
cups on the patch, I should say, were quite
numerous, though the daisies were few. ’I hat was
the one curious tiling. And the other was that
L —— was reprimanded by his Honor the Lieut.-
Governor. Van Dieman’s Land was still a depen-
dency of the old colony, and Colonel Arthur did
not become His Excellency for a year afterwards.
And what do you think the reprimand was for ?
Because L was promoting dissension among
prisoners of Irish birth or extraction by letting it
be known that some of the “ould soil” was in
our garden ! It appeared that a constable in the
Lumber Yard—that’s where the gang-labourers
were barracked at night—overheal’d some of the
Irish prisoners planning a bolt so that they might
come and kiss the sod ! My husband only made
his peace by stating that when he found the soil
was Irish, and not English, he felt disgusted.
That was the truth —but I felt the same. It
was a childish and unreasoning racial prejudice, but
I never loved these simple flowers so much after I
knew they grew on Irish ground, and not on
English. It was not so much that they were not
English buds, as that they were Irish. And if it
was weak of my husband, you must bear in mind
that at that time, and for long subsequently, the
word of the Lieutenant-Governor was law.
Garrison Orders and Governors’ Proclamations
were all the Acts of Parliament we had till 1820,
and things were said and done by persons in the
highest colonial authority then that would not be
tolerated from the Czar of Russia now. My hus-
band used to say of the Government that it was
a despotism distempered by dyspepsia and dissipa-
tion. Times hadn’t changed much from the day
when it was no uncommon occurrence for all
adult males, from the Governor downward to the
newest landed convict to be drunk together. Men
were flogged so that commanding-officers might
win a wager made as to their “gameness or
capacity of endurance under suffering, torture was
applied to wring confessions out of supposed
criminals, and one man, at least, was hanged on
his own confession so obtained who, it was proved
later, was innocent of the crime to which he had
confessed !
Ah, those days ! those days ! Life was very
bright for myself and my husband then in most
things; no shadow was on our lot, save the shadow
of others’ sufferings. And how deep that was,
we, of the olden time, dare not say. It blackened
existence for us who tried to retain our
humanity, or, as the others said, keep our soft-
heartedness. Not that we did not harden, too.
We had to harden or else we should have had to
resign or —or, go mad. .
My husband often talked of resigning, though
we had no money to settle down in another
pursuit, but then the thought would occur to him
that In' could do a little to stem the current of
cruelty and injustice, and that perhaps his suc-
cessor might not have the wM to do that. For
nothing was more horrible to witness than the
de-humanising influence of the System on its
administrators. From the highest to the lowest,
there was scarcely an exception— scarcely a man,
or woman either, who did not plainly deteriorate.
The contact with criminals, do you say ? No, Jhe
exercise of arbitrary, irresponsible power. Give
men, anywhere, and of any race, power, with no
external antlioiity to call them to account, and
infect them with the idea that their duty is to
punish their fellow creatures, and their natures
will change. They may retain the semblance of
brave men and fair women, but they will petrify
from the heart outwards. But lam getting away
from my story, and the buttercups and daisies.
# # *
Well, just as the last yellow-cupped thing
shrivelled up, word came from the Lieutenant-
Governor that L , my husband, was to go
down to Macquarie Harbour by the Cyprus.
There was some Commissariat business that re-
quired investigation, I think. The Harbour
records had become muddled —it was a way all
early records had, as you must have learnt—and
it was necessary for someone to visit the Harbour
to adjust them. The Commandant at the Settle-
ment was unusually punctilious. When his
accounts became mixed, he hesitated to adopt the
common colonial method of adjusting them. He
did not throw them into the fire. Oh, dear, what
a lot of trouble that practice has saved ! But the
Lieutenant in command was either too innocent or
lacked the courage to adopt the usual plan.
I forget the precise difficulty into which he had
fallen. There was a discrepancy, I know, between
the number of prisoners he ought to have had on
hand, and those actually under control. But that
was nothing. The System never bothered its
head about half-a-dozen convicts more or less. It
was so easy, you see, to write them off as
“escaped ”or “ dead.” It was only when it came
to such things as fetters, and shirts, and hand-
cuffs, or tin-plates that the System grew parti-
cular. L told me once that over a hundred
letters had passed between the Storekeeper in
Hobart Town, the Keeper of the House of Cor-
rection, Launceston, and a Secretary of State’s
Department in London—Treasury or Colonies—
over a surcharged dozen pairs of handcuffs.. But
dear, dear, how I’m rambling on! I must get
back to the Harbour—and the buttercups.
* * *
I suppose, then, it had something to do
with the stores, my husband’s trip. Parson
Knopwood came out to dine with us the very
night that L brought me word he was ordered
to go. 1 did not like being left alone in my
cottage for, perhaps, two or three months. My
menage was not quite so bad as Mrs. Pnnsep’s,
who wrote home that her cook had committed
murder, her footman burglary, and her housemaid
bigamy, but it was quite bad enough. I had only
two servants, both women, and certainly one
of these had committed murder. It was the old
story of man’s treachery. She killed her child in
a moment of frenzy. The women were faithful to
me and I could do*anything with them, but then
our cottage was lonely—and, in short, I made such
a fuss that L resolved to take me with him if
he could obtain permission.
“I’m in his Honor’s black-books, you know,
Jess,” he said, “ over that piece of the ‘ould sod,’
and it is uncertain whether I’ll succeed. Indeed,
I’m not sure that this journey is not a punishment
for my offence in letting people know about the
thing.”
“Look you here, Mrs. L ,” cried the
“Ancient,” “you shall go; I promise you that.
I haven’t asked a favour for many a day, and I’ll
ask this. But, to make sure, I’ll tell you what
I’ll do. Give me permission to dig up that square
of turf from your garden, and then Mr. L can
tell Colonel Arthur it has been removed.”
“You cunning man 1” I laughed, “ you wish to
get it all yourself !” For now that I saw the value
that others placed on that bit of earth, the senti-
ment —perhaps you will call it sentimentality—
that bad warmed me when I first saw the country
flowers again took possession of me, and I was
loth to let others have it.
“ Tush, ma’am he was very brusque some-
times, was the old minister —“I shall replace it
after you’ve gone! Combine the wisdom of the
serpent, ma’am, with the harmlessness of the
dove ! Excuse my preaching—l don’t often preach
out of the pulpit—not half enough, folks say, and
to tell the truth I don’t see much use preaching at
all here. As well preach in Gehenna.
So it was arranged, and that very night, about
ten o’clock, L and the old chaplain dug from
the plot as near as possible a square yard—rather
over than under. But they were clumsier than
the doctor, for the block broke in several irregular-
edged lumps. Dear, how we laughed over it, but
I cried, too, before we finished the task.
One of the maids, not the murderess, but the
other, a South of Ireland girl, saw us through the
attic-window—it was bright moonlight. She
called to us, and begged us by Holy Mary and
several Saints to give her a morsel of the “swate
ould land.” I had locked her and the other maid
in their room at nine o’clock, as was our custom
as per regulation, and she could not get down to-
us as we thought. I teased her mightily, and the
silly thing offered me all her money just for a bit
of the sod. I asked her how much she had, and
she answerednine shillings and threepence in notes.
You know there was very little coin of any
sort in circulation in those days, and I had given

her at odd times some of the promissory-notes for
trifling sums—threepences, sixpences, and shil-
lings—which the Hobart Town tradesmen issued
in lieu of cash change. She offered me all her
store ; she’d do anything just for a fragment. She
pleaded so piteously that had I not been simply
teasing her, I should have relented. As it was,
the tears came into my eyes as her appeal
revealed to us the intensity of genuine emotion in
the poor, ignorant girl’s nature. I told her that I
should save her a lump, and.,she was invoking
countless blessings on my unworthy head when
the parson interjected, as a joke, of course, “ that
if she wanted a piece she would have to come for
it then and there. ”
Now, that girl was like nearly all the daughters
of her class and Faith, as modest as—as any
English maiden of the Sunday-school story-books.
It is a strange thing to say, but it’s a true thing.
An Irish girl-transport, however criminal in other
respects, was seldom immodest. She would have
to sink very low indeed before she became
shameless.
That particular girl was one of the most modest
women I ever met in any circle. And yet, on
hearing Mr. Knopwood’s words, and although she
was attired simply in her white night dress, and
before we apprehended what was her intention,
she had got out of the attic window (it projected
over the gable of the porch) and climbed on to the
porch-roof, and thence descended to the ground.
In another moment she had grasped a double
handful of the soil, and then, her situation flash-
ing upon her, she rushed back into the house.
My husband looked at me—the moonlight was
so vivid that I could see his eyes dancing with
mirthfulness—but the old clergyman kept his
fixed on the ground. “Poor girl! poor girl !” I
heard him say to himself. And then he turned
to me and said : “ Ma’am, this thing opens up a
new view to me, and Bobby Knopwood isn’t too
old to learn, although he is 62 years of age.”
“ Well, Parson,” I said, as I was about to follow
the girl into the house, “what have you learnt ?”
Now, the old Chaplain was very Ash-Wednes-
dayish when he was deeply moved, and I was not,
therefore, at all surprised to hear him say, “ That
we’re all cursed fools ! ”
I paused on the porch-steps, and, making him a
mock-courtesy, returned that I was indebted for
his good opinion of me.
“Oh, I don’t mean you,” he replied ; “I mean
the Authorities.”
“ Still I don’t understand,” I was proceeding to
say; when he burst out: “No, you don’t under-
stand ;of course you don’t. And won’t! What
I mean is that when England transports her
criminals she does so with the distinctly-stated
intention of destroying every tie which binds
them to their native land. What the System at
home dictates the System here obeys. Don’t
you see what folly, what wickedness, it all is?
Look at that girl there—running the risk of severe
punishment for a lump of dirt and a tussock of
grass! There’s an affectioiTwhich should have been
encouraged rather than repressed. And now that
I think of it, hundreds of instances of the kind
have come under my own eyes, but I did not
understand their significance before. Why, if you
and L and I made donkeys of ourselves over
those trumpery flowers merely because they re-
minded us of our childhood, why, I say, shouldn’t
these poor wretches feel the same impulse ? Are
we three any the worse for that sensation ? Would
they be wickeder? Might not they be all the
better for it? But there, there—l’m preaching
again like an old fool. I’ll stop, else you’ll think
I’ve been having too much wine. Good-night,
good-night!”
I was astonished at his tirade, as you may guess,
and it set me thinking. What was more to the
purpose, however, he spoke to that effect to Gover-
nor Arthur—not, however, till after he had sup-
ported my husband’s application for permission to
take me with him in the Cyprus.
And now it is quite time I got to the point of
my story.
The Cyprus was in Sullivan’s Cove waiting final
orders. I was on board, but my husband was to
come off with the last boat. I was chatting with
an officer of the military guard—we were taking
down over a score of the vilest characters of the
Island, and there were an officer and ten men to
guard them—when we heard the sentry of the
gangway challenge a boat. “A friend—Knop-
wood, Chaplain,” was the reply. Military routine,
you know, was always strictly observed when
prisoners were under guard. The sentry re-
sponded : “ Pass, friend, and give the counter-
tigu,” and as the officer of the guard went towards
the gangway I heard the sound of oars as the boat
was brought alongside, and, in another minute,
Mr. Knopwood was standing by me. “Look you
here, Mrs. L ,” he said, “ I’ve brought you
this at the risk of offending his High and Mighti-
ness ” (that was one of his ways of alluding to the
Governor). Now, you will never guess what that
was? It was simply a lump of the “ould sod.” I
asked him what I was to do with it, and he told
me to take it down to Macquarie Harbour, and
make an experiment with some poor God-forsaken
devil of an Irishman—these were his very words !
—and I was to let him know what happened. Of
course I took it, and so began what L called
the procession of the butter-cup.
# # •
1 will not bore you with an account of our
voyage down, as it was uneventful, save for our
being weather-locked in Recherche Bay, the very
place where the same was seized by the
mutineers a few years subsequently. We got to
the harbour at last after a three weeks’ trip,
during which I was ill nearly the whole time.
Neither shall I particularly describe to you the
Harbour or the prison-settlement as it then was.
All I need to say is that what Norfolk Islandjwas
ten years later to both the colonies, Macquarie
Harbour was at this time to V.D.L.—the recep-
tacle for her most desperate criminals. I had not
read Dante when I made my visit. When, lofig
after, I read those terrible Cantos, beginning with
the eighteenth, I thought that I, too, had been in
the cloisters of hell! lam sure of this—that in
the inner circles of Hell will the name of Mac-
quarie Harbour be held in honour! God forgive
the nation that constructed that Infekno in these
fair Southern Seas ! (The old lady was silent
here for some minutes.)
No man dare more than hint at the horrors
which were the common things of every-day life
in that awful place; no woman dare even as
Ir y^ c ‘ l as hint. Seven superior military and civil
officers, forty inferior officers and soldiers, and
two hundred and eighty prisoners, including some
twenty women, formed the population.
I would never have set foot in that fearful
place, had I had the faintest conception of its true
nature. Looking back now, and believing that
J did a little good there, I yet would have
bade him throw up his appointment in the
Establishment, rather than he should have been
brought face to face with the Hell’s Gates of
Macquarie Harbour. It forced him to make the
acquaintance of such concrete depravity as only
Lucifer could dream of.
In this dreadful spot, God-forsaken and Church
forsaken for the System could not afford to pay
a lun d r ®d a y ear to a minister of the Gospel,
though it was receiving a thousand pounds
annually, clear profit from the sale of the timber
cut by the Harbour prisoners, we (my husband
and myself) lived for twelve months. L—nr-, on
examining Captain B ’s accounts, found such
serious discrepancies that he recommended the
Captain to go at once to the capital and make his
peace, and offered to stay at the Harbour while he
was absent. My husband’s orders authorised him
to suspend the Captain if he considered it desir-
able, but he did not take the extreme course, and
preferred, rather than that B—-- should be ruined,
to subject ourselves to some inconvenience. When
."T suggested this, neither of us knew all the
infamies of Hell’s Gates, or else B would have
had to take his chance.
Well, B went away with the Cyprus as soon
as the schooner could be loaded with pine, and we
stopped. The officers soon returned to their old
practices, which they had temporarily abandoned
on our arrival, fearful of L ’s report.
The sod had been planted by my own hands, in
front of our quarters at Sarah Island, the day
after my landing. A convict, allotted to us as a
servant, looked wonderingly at me. I thought it
a good time to begin the parson’s experiment, and
so 1 mentioned to him what it was. The fellow
openly sneered in my face, and, going away to
another corner of the barrack-yard, spoke of me in
ribald terms, so that I could not help but hear. A
soldier also heard him and reported the man’s
conduct, and, without any reference to me, he was
ordered fifty lashes. The lash went regularly
morning and evening. They would order a man
fifty, but as the dose, given all at once, would
incapacitate him from work that day, they would
bestow half in the morning and half at night on
his return to the barracks, Well, my servant for
his impertinence got his fifty in two instalments,
morning and night. After the latter, he must
have dragged himself to the grassplot, and dug up
the sod. It was a sad beginning, I thought, for
my experiment.
The next morning I saw the hole. What drew
my attention to it first was a group of billet
convicts and soldiers, apparently all Irishmen,
eight or ten in number, gathered round the spot!
Then one of each class came to my quarters and
asked to see me. L went out to enquire what
they wanted. “Was it true that the lady had
carried down with her a bit of Old Ireland ?” It
was. “Would the lady mind coming out and
seeing if it was that sod that had been taken ?”
I went out, and noticed that that particular lump
had gone, and, reluctantly, said so. With a yell,
the whole.group ran excitedly across the angle of
the yard, and in a few moments returned, hauling
something with them. It was my convict servant!
Those fellows, soldiers as well 'as pursuers, were
mad with rage. Not at the offence to me, mark
you, but at the insult to the country in
which they had been born ! L and a
military officer were compelled to exercise their
authority before the enraged men would release
the poor wretch who had vented his spite on the
bit of earth. It appeared he had thrown the lump
on the sandy beach. One of the soldiers ran
and found the soil there scattered in fragments.
He gathered them up in his cap and brought I
them to me. I asked him if he would like a tuft. I
He was an old man, weazened and gray, but his ■
face flushed as I spoke. I thought it was in
gratitude. But it was for shame. He put out
his hand, and then drew it sharply back ; “Oi
won’t loi about ut, Me’m. Oi ’av’ a bit o’ the
dirt.” He had pocketed a piece !
“ Oh, the yelling and the crying of the others
when they saw that capful of soil. They did not
stop to ask whether it was the same that I had
carried all the way from Hobart Town, but if it
had not been, I should, when I saw their joy, i
have perpetrated a pious fraud, and declared it
was, I was about to hand them the lot when
told me to see if any roots were among the
soil. I looked, I could not see the daisy-root,
but I found the round bulb of the buttercup!
“ We’ll keep that for our share, dear,”L said,
and took it and a little of the soil in his fingers.
A convict saw him and, recognising the root!
shouted: “Luk at him now! If that ain’t a
Saint Anthony’s turnip !” Then there was more
shouting, and some crying, and some others
speaking as if they saw before them a whole field
of the yellow gold-cups.
* * •
As I have said, we were there some
months—-dreary, terrible months they were—and
then September came again, ushered in by terrible
storms that lashed the harbour into hills and
gullies of foam. News came by means of the
patrol-boat that one of the two men confined to
the rock on Grummet Island—just as Marcus
Clarke describes Rufus Dawes to have been—
had been so storm-beaten that he was
dying. You must know the cells on that
small island were hewn out of the rock
and were invaded by the waves in a rough
sea, and in truth this prisoner had had the life
literally flogged out of him by the lash of the wave.
It was a cruel death, but perhaps it was most
merciful after all. Ho was such a desperate ruffian
that it was only a matter of time for him to die
at the triangles or on the scaffold. Well, the cox-
patrol-boat reported the man’s con-
dition to the Acting-Commandant—that is, to my
husband, and, of course, he ordered the dying
wretch to be transferred to the mainland. L -
went to see him in the Barracks. He repelled him
with awful words. Then a hospital wardsraan
told him the story of the Irish sod. The bits of
earth were treasured, let mo say, like diamonds.
The dying man begged that a morsel of the “ould
land ” would bo buried with him. But no com-
patriot would consent to part with as much as a
crumb. And then and then— ho sent to me a
message : He’d let me pray with him if I would
only grant hie request!
I had put the bit of earth round the buttercup-
root, and had planted the whole in a window-box
with some native soil. The convict’s message re-
minded me of it. I examined the box—and there,
just peeping from the surface, was a tiny, green
spike. It was the buttercup.
I went to that man’s bedside. I was Protestant
—he was a Catholic; so I said “Our Father” and
the “Hail Mary” to him. But he would not
listen till I gave him the crumbs of earth. That
was the first time I visited him.
When I went next he said : “There’s that king-
cup a-comin’ up, mem, an’ I won’t die till ut’s
a-blowin’ an’ a-shinin’, so I tells yez that.” It
was astounding how news of the most trifling
things permeated the barracks. He had been told
of that little spike ! In fact, the whole of the
prisoners knew of it—at Sarah Island and Grum-
met Rock, and the gravediggers on the two burial
islands, for, as I learnt afterwards, one of the
soldiers had watched the box daily.
He didn’t die for some weeks, though, such was
his weakness it was a miracle he lived. But he
kept his word ; he didn’t die till the first yellow-
bloom nodded on a feeble stem.
I did not go to see him one morning. At noon
to me : “ Mem, the King-cup’s a-blowin’,
an’ I want to see ut, an’ die,” was his message. I
went in the afternoon, and my convict-servant—
the one I spoke of—carried the window-box. As
I entered the hospital ward, the sentry saluted as
usual. The convict-servant drew the man’s atten-
tion to the flower in the box. Now that soldier
was not an Irishman ; he was an Englishman, but
he presented arms again. I turned and asked
him why he did so. He answered, sheepishly, “ I
was silly, ma’am, but I couldn’t help it; I saluted
the flower!”
The sick man was almost at the last gasp. That
was evident. He rallied, however, momentarily
when he saw me.
“’Av you brought the flower, mem ?’’he whis-
pered, and tried to raise himself. I motioned my
servant to place the box by the bedside. The
dying man bent over and cried, “Luk, luk at
that grane grass. There’s no grane loike that in
this damned counthry. Oh, the grane o’ the
blessed owld land ! An’ the orange, too —the
orange an’ the grane a-blowin’ an’ a-growin’
together. Fwhat ’ud Father Kernan say .”
He fell back, muttering to himself. I leant j
over him and thought it was the “ Hail, Mary ”
he was repeating. But it was not —nor would you
ever guess what it was !
It was :
Buttercups and daisies,
O, the pretty flowers !
*# . *
I was to hear the childish song once more
before I left the Harbour—in death accents of
another kind.
The schooner arrived the very next day with a
new Commandant, and instructions for my hus-
band to resign authority and return forthwith.
The_ new ruler was a man in whom the extremes
of high birth and bestial cruelty met. He was in
charge a week when one of the timber-gangs mur-
dered an overseer. It was the old story—murder
to obtain freedom by being themselves hanged!
They were tried by three officers and eight were
sentenced to be hanged the next day at nine
o’clock. Now, a Macquarie Harbour execution
was a synonym for ribaldry and blasphemy, and
the most shameful scene was anticipated on this
particular occasion.
Overnight my husband saw the Condemned.
There were four of each faith. He said to
Catholics one by one : “If you will conduct your-
self decently, I’ll tell you what I’ll do—l’ll take
care a piece of Old Ireland is buried with you.”
Each promised.
To the four Protestants he said : “Now, men,
I’ve done what I could to lighten your lot, haven’t
I?” They assented. “ I’d like to do you another
kindness, if you’ll allow me?” Of course they
would. “What was it?” Will you consent to
my wife coming and placing a flower, an English
flower, ” He was interrupted by one of the
Condemned saying: “Curse the English flower,
curse England, England was as bad as a step-
mother to him 1” My husband took no notice of
the remark, but another of the Condemned struck
the speaker across the mouth. “Shut up, you
fool,” said the latter fellow, “you ain’t worth the
honour the leddy intends doin’ you !”
Then the same man went on to speak to my
husband : “You mean, sir, the missus’ll come an’
put the flower on our graves?” “No,” said
E > “on your breasts, in—in your coffins!”
“And what do you want?” asked the man ; “you
wouldn’t let yer lady come a-near us without you
had some objec’. What is it ? You want us to die
quiet ?”
Of course, my husband said that was his inten-
tion.
“ Well,” said the condemned, “I’m willing, if
my pals are.” The rest had no objection after that.
The next morning I waited trembling, by my
window till I should hear the thud. I abhorred
the awful duty my husband had imposed upon me,
but I was determined to perform it. To have
evaded it would.have been to crush aside for ever
his influence with every convict in the whole
colony, for the news of the breach of faith would
have spread even from Macquarie Harbour. I had
dressed myself in black. The only speck of bright
colour about me was the hue of the four butter-
cups, the last of the blooms, which I held in my
hand.
I sat, with tense faculties, craving, yet fearing,
to hear the hateful sound. I heard a chant. It
was not a hymn—of that I felt sure—but for some
moments I could not distinguish the words. Then
I knew: It was the children’s song of the “But-
tercups and Daisies.” I burst into tears. I was
still crying when, half-an-hour later, my husband
came for me. I thanked God for those tears, for
they blinded me so that I did not see above the
circlets of ropes. The eight coffins were in a row
—that much I perceived—but my husband had to
guide my hand to—to the resting-place for—my
flowers. And that was the last stage but one of
the procession of my buttercups.
